Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
Does Subsequent Ownership or Marriage After Zina Waive the Hadd?
Intra-Hanafi dispute with ta'lil on hadd waiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi positions on whether subsequent purchase or marriage after zina waives the hadd, and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind the distinction between milk al-'ayn and milk al-intifa'.

Core Idea
Purchase after zina may waive hadd through shubhah; marriage does not, because only purchase grants full ownership.

One might think that any subsequent lawful relationship can erase the punishment for prior zina. The Hanafi masters, however, debate this carefully. This lesson walks through a classic intra-Hanafi dispute recorded by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Rai'q, where the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani are weighed, and a powerful analogy to theft is used to distinguish between purchase and marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key cases and their rulings
Locate the two main scenarios: (1) zina with a slave-girl then purchase, (2) zina with a woman then marriage. Note the conflicting reports about which imam said what.
The source contains multiple transmissions; do not get lost in the narrations—focus on the final distinction.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
List the positions attributed to Abu Hanifa (multiple reports), Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Identify which view is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah and which in Nawadir.
The author resolves the dispute by preferring the view that distinguishes purchase from marriage.
3
Analyze the ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Understand the core argument: purchase grants milk al-'ayn (ownership of the entity), while marriage grants only milk al-intifa' (right of enjoyment). This difference makes purchase analogous to the theft case where ownership before amputation waives the hadd.
The analogy to theft is crucial—the same illa (full ownership) applies.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: in a fatwa, the judge would apply hadd if the man marries after zina, but not if he purchases the slave-girl after zina (according to the mufta bihi position).
This distinction is operative in Hanafi courts.
5
Apply the ruling to a variant
Consider a scenario where a man usurps a slave-girl, commits zina, then guarantees her value (as mentioned at the beginning). Does the same reasoning apply?
The source indicates that guarantee is treated like purchase because it leads to full ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : رجل غصب جارية وزنى بها ثم ضمن قيمتها فلا حد عليه، وعلى قياس قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يسقط الحد، وعلى قياس ما روي عن أبي يوسف ينبغي أن يسقط كما يذكر التي تليه .اهـ. رجل زنى بأمة ثم اشتراها ذكر في ظاهر الرواية أنه يحد، وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يسقط الحد، وذكر أصحاب الإملاء عن أبي يوسف أن من زنى بامرأة ثم تزوجها أو بأمة ثم اشتراها لا حد عليه عند أبي حنيفة وعليه الحد في قول أبي يوسف. في المسألة في وذكر ابن سماعة في نوادره على عكس هذا وقال : وعلى قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد عليه الحد الوجهين، وعن أبي يوسف لا حد عليه في الوجهين، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه إذا زنى بأمة ثم اشتراها فلا حد عليه وإن زنى بامرأة ثم تزوجها فعليه الحد. والفرق بين النكاح والشراء أنه بالشراء يملك عينها وملك العين في محل الحل سبب لملك الحل فيجعل الطارئ قبل الاستيفاء كالمقترن بالسبب كما في باب السرقة فإن السارق إذا ملك المسروق قبل القطع يمتنع القطع فأما بالنكاح فلا يملك عين المرأة وإنما ثبت له ملك الاستيفاء، ولهذا لو وطئت المنكوحة بشبهة كان العقر لها فلا يورث ذلك شبهة فيما تقدم استيفاؤه منها فلا يسقط الحد عنه.
And in al-Dhahiriyya: A man who usurps a slave-girl and commits zina with her then guarantees her value, there is no hadd upon him. And by qiyas of the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the hadd is not dropped. And by qiyas of what is related from Abu Yusuf, it should be dropped as mentioned in what follows. A man committed zina with a slave-girl then purchased her. It is mentioned in the Zahir al-Riwayah that he is punished. And it is related from Abu Yusuf that the hadd is dropped. And the scholars of al-Imla' related from Abu Yusuf that whoever commits zina with a woman then marries her, or with a slave-girl then purchases her, there is no hadd upon him according to Abu Hanifa, while according to Abu Yusuf there is hadd upon him. Ibn Sama'a in his Nawadir related the opposite, saying: According to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, there is hadd upon him in both cases. And from Abu Yusuf, there is no hadd upon him in both cases. And al-Hasan related from Abu Hanifa that if he commits zina with a slave-girl and then purchases her, there is no hadd upon him. And if he commits zina with a woman and then marries her, there is hadd upon him. And the difference between marriage and purchase is that by purchase he owns her very self, and ownership of the self in a place of permissibility is a cause for ownership of permissibility. Thus the supervening event before execution is treated like concurrent with the cause, as in the chapter of theft: if the thief owns the stolen property before amputation, amputation is prevented. As for marriage, he does not own the woman's self, but rather ownership of enjoyment is established for him. Therefore, if the married woman is penetrated due to shubhah, the dower is for her, so that does not create shubhah regarding what has already been enjoyed from her, so the hadd is not dropped from him.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether subsequent acquisition of ownership or marital rights after committing zina can waive the hadd punishment. The discussion begins with a case from al-Dhahiriyya: a man usurps a slave-girl, commits zina, then guarantees her value (presumably becoming owner). According to the source, the hadd is dropped. However, by qiyas according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the hadd is not dropped, while according to Abu Yusuf it should be dropped. The author then presents the central case: a man commits zina with a slave-girl and later purchases her. According to the Zahir al-Riwaya, he is punished (the hadd is applied). But Abu Yusuf reports that the hadd is dropped. Another transmission (from the scholars of al-Imla') states that according to Abu Hanifa, if someone commits zina with a woman and later marries her, or with a slave-girl and later purchases her, the hadd is dropped; whereas Abu Yusuf holds that the hadd is imposed. Ibn Sama'a in his Nawadir reverses this, attributing the dropping of hadd to Abu Yusuf in both cases and imposing it on Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Al-Hasan's narration from Abu Hanifa distinguishes: purchase drops the hadd, marriage does not. The author then provides the legal reasoning (ta'lil) for the difference between marriage and purchase. Purchase transfers full ownership of the slave-girl's person, and ownership of the object in a permissible context is a cause for the permissibility of use. Therefore, the subsequent ownership is treated as if it existed at the time of intercourse, analogous to theft: if the thief owns the stolen item before amputation, amputation is prevented. In marriage, the husband does not own the woman's person but only the right to enjoy her. Thus, if the married woman was approached under shubhah (e.g., mistaken identity), the dower is for her, and this does not create a shubhah regarding the earlier unlawful act, so the hadd remains. The passage illustrates the jadal (debate) methodology of the Hanafi school, where each imam's position is presented with its underlying qiyas and istihsan, and the final mufta bihi position is determined by the weight of evidence.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes like zina (unlawful sexual intercourse).
شبهة (shubhah): Legal ambiguity that raises doubt about the applicability of a punishment, such as a mistaken belief of permissibility or a defect in the legal grounds.
ملك العين (milk al-'ayn): Ownership of the physical entity itself, as opposed to mere usufruct (milk al-intifa').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason that purchasing a slave-girl after zina may waive the hadd, but marrying a free woman after zina does not?
A) Because marriage is a contract of usufruct, not full ownership
B) Because the hadd for zina with a free woman is more severe
C) Because marriage requires witnesses while purchase does not
D) Because the free woman has a guardian

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, subsequent purchase of a slave-girl after zina creates a shubhah that waives the hadd, but subsequent marriage does not, because only purchase grants full ownership (milk al-'ayn).

Shubha in the Act: The Case of the Threefold Divorce
Explaining the condition of shubha in the act and its application to the triple divorce case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts a person from Hadd for intercourse with a thrice-divorced woman during her iddah, and evaluate the Zahiri objection.

Core Idea
A legal remnant creates shubha that averts Hadd, even when the act is known to be haram.

This passage examines a pivotal case in Hanafi Hadd law: whether a man who has intercourse with his thrice-divorced wife during her iddah receives the prescribed punishment. While the act is undoubtedly haram, the Hanafi school holds that remaining legal effects—like maintenance and dwelling—create a legal ambiguity (shubha) that prevents the imposition of Hadd. Understanding how the author establishes this ruling will deepen your grasp of the doctrine of shubha and its role in criminal law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the rule from the matn: no Hadd if the act is accompanied by shubha, illustrated by the thrice-divorced woman. Note the condition that the person thought the act was halal.
The passage later argues even knowledge doesn't trigger Hadd.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
The Zahiri school claims ijma' that three divorces are effective, so no shubha exists.
They assert the evidence is definitive.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Zahiris rely on the claim of consensus among the Companions after Umar's enforcement.
This would make the matter qati'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi author cites a hadith that three divorces were counted as one in early Islam, showing the matter is not qati'. He also notes that the Zahiris themselves dissent, breaking any claimed ijma'.
This leaves room for shubha.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If shubha is recognized, Hadd is averted; otherwise, it is imposed. The Hanafi position leads to no punishment, while the Zahiri view would punish.
The author reconciles two conflicting references in al-Hidayah by distinguishing between one-word and separate pronouncements.

The Book of Hadd: Chapter on Intercourse that Necessitates Hadd and that which Does Not. 'And due to shubha in the act if he thought it was halal, like the woman observing iddah after triple divorce, and the slave-girl of his parents, and his wife, and his master, and lineage is established.' This is his statement: '(And due to shubha in the act if he thought it was halal, like the woman observing iddah after triple divorce, and the slave-girl of his parents, and his wife, and his master)' meaning: no Hadd because of shubha in the act, on condition that he thought the intercourse was halal, because ownership and right are not established in the type, because the prohibition of the thrice-divorced woman is definitive, so no ownership or right remains for him in her, except that some rulings remain, like maintenance, dwelling, preventing her from leaving, establishment of lineage, prohibition of her sister and four others, and inadmissibility of testimony of each for the other, so ambiguity occurred due to that, thus it caused shubha when he thought it was halal, because it is a place of ambiguity, so he is excused. He made it general in the triple divorce, including whether he pronounced it in one session or separately. And no consideration is given to the dissent of one who denies occurrence in one session because it contradicts the definitive; thus the commentators mentioned. But there is consideration because of what is in Sahih Muslim: that triple divorce was one in the time of the Prophet ﷺ and Abu Bakr and the early part of Umar's caliphate, until Umar made it three upon the people. Even though scholars answered and interpreted it, the evidence for occurrence of three in one sentence is not definitive. If it is said: the scholars have consensus on it, we say: the Zahiris opposed in that, as they transmitted in the book of Talaq. So it is appropriate that no Hadd be imposed even if he knew the prohibition. The evidence for it is what al-Hidayah mentioned from the book of Nikah in the chapter on prohibited women: that Hadd is not obligatory by intercourse with a woman divorced a ba'in divorce, one or three, with knowledge of the prohibition, according to the indication of the book of Talaq. According to the wording of the book of Hudud, it is obligatory because ownership has ceased in the intercourse from the right of permissibility, so zina is verified. End. And it is appropriate that the indication of the book of Talaq be carried upon when he pronounced it with one word, and the wording of the book of Hudud upon when he pronounced it separately, as we mentioned, as a reconciliation between them, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the condition of shubha (legal ambiguity) in the act of intercourse that averts the Hadd punishment. The author is explaining the rule from the matn: no Hadd is imposed if there is shubha in the act itself, provided the person believed the act was halal. The first example given is a woman divorced three times (mu'taddat al-thalath). The author explains that although her prohibition is definitive (majmu' bih), certain legal effects remain (e.g., maintenance, dwelling, lineage) which create ambiguity, leading to shubha. The discussion then focuses on the triple divorce case: if the divorce was pronounced in one sitting, the author questions the claim that the evidence is definitive (qati'), citing a hadith from Sahih Muslim that during the Prophet's time and early caliphate, three divorces were considered one, until Umar made them three. The author argues that this shows the matter is not qati', and thus shubha exists. He refutes the objection that there is ijma' on the effectiveness of three divorces by noting that the Zahiris disagreed. Therefore, even if the person knew the prohibition, no Hadd is imposed due to shubha. The author reconciles two conflicting references in al-Hidayah: the Nikah book indicates no Hadd, while the Hudud book indicates Hadd. He resolves this by considering that the Nikah book's indication applies when the divorce was pronounced in one word (making it similar to a single ba'in divorce), and the Hudud book's wording applies when it was pronounced separately. Thus, the preferred view is that no Hadd is imposed in either case.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Legal ambiguity that averts the Hadd punishment, arising from remaining rights or effects despite the clear prohibition.
معتدة الثلاث (mu'taddat al-thalath): A woman observing the iddah period after a triple talāq (three divorces). Despite the divorce being final, certain rights like maintenance and dwelling remain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hadd is wajib
B) No Hadd due to shubha
C) Ta'zir only
D) It depends on the number of words used in divorce

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's broad application of shubha to avert Hadd, even when the prohibition is known, exemplifies the legal maxim 'al-hadd yudra'u bil-shubuhat'.

Further Cases of Shubha: Slaves, Mukhalla'ah, and the Mortgaged Slave-Girl
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the mortgaged slave-girl case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on Hadd for intercourse with slave-girls in cases of shubha and identify the contested case of the mortgaged slave-girl, explaining the two riwayas and the preferred view.

Core Idea
A mortgagee's intercourse with a mortgaged slave-girl is like zina if he knows it's haram—shubha does not excuse deliberate sin.

This lesson examines three additional cases where shubha may or may not avert the Hadd punishment for zina: the slave-girl of one's parents, the woman divorced by khul' (mukhalla'ah), and the mortgaged slave-girl (marhoonah). The passage builds on the principle that shubha must be genuine; a claim of ignorance after knowing the prohibition does not create shubha. The focus is on the internal Hanafi disagreement about the mortgaged slave-girl, where two authoritative riwayas exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
List the three categories: slave-girl of parents/wife/master, the mukhalla'ah (woman divorced by khul'), and the mortgaged slave-girl (marhoonah). Note that each has a different basis for potential shubha.
2
Determine the ruling for the first two cases
For the slave-girl of parents, if the man thought it was halal due to customary permission, no Hadd. For the mukhalla'ah, if he knew the prohibition, Hadd is wajib; if he genuinely thought it was halal, no Hadd.
The mukhalla'ah case distinguishes between khul' with and without the word 'khul'.
3
Analyze the two riwayas for the mortgaged slave-girl
In the riwaya of the book of Rahn, no Hadd because the mortgage creates a right to the value, generating shubha. In the riwaya of the book of Hudud, Hadd is wajib because the mortgagee's right is only in the value, not the 'ayn.
Both riwayas are from Zahir al-riwayah.
4
Understand the tarjih and its rationale
Al-Hidayah and al-Tabyin declare the Hudud riwaya as al-ashah (most correct) and al-mukhtar (chosen). The rationale: the mortgagee cannot use the 'ayn itself, so intercourse is like zina. However, if he genuinely thought it halal, no Hadd by agreement.
A third, minority riwaya from al-Idah says Hadd even if he thought it halal, but it is rejected.

As for intercourse with the slave-girl of his parents, and his wife, and his master: he has no ownership or right of ownership in her, except that familiarity (al-busuta) exists among them in benefiting from wealth and satisfaction with that customarily, and it is permissible to benefit from wealth legally. So if he thought [it was halal] of this kind, he is excused because intercourse with slave-girls is of the type of using [them] and the situation becomes ambiguous, and ambiguity in its place is excusable. These issues have sisters, among them: the woman divorced in exchange for money (al-mukhall'a bi mal), because her prohibition is established by consensus, so she becomes like the thrice-divorced woman; thus the commentators mentioned. And they mean divorce in exchange for money not with the word khul'. As for if it was with the word khul', we have already mentioned the difference concerning it, and that the Companions differed about it. But in al-Bada'i': 'If he divorced her by khul' or divorced her in exchange for money and then had intercourse with her during the iddah, al-Karkhi mentioned that the ruling should be like the ruling for the thrice-divorced woman.' And that is correct because the removal of ownership by khul' and divorce in exchange for money is agreed upon, so shubha is not realized, and Hadd is obligatory unless he claims ambiguity. And among them is the umm walad when her master emancipates her, due to the establishment of her prohibition by consensus, and shubha is established upon ambiguity due to the remaining effect of the marital bed (al-firash), which is the iddah. And among them is the mortgaged slave-girl (al-jariya al-marhoona) with respect to the mortgagee (al-murtahin) in the transmission (riwaya) of the book of Hudud. So if the mortgagee says, 'I knew it was haram and had intercourse with her,' then there are two riwayas: in the riwaya of the book of Rahn, no Hadd on him, and it is of the first type as we mentioned; and in the riwaya of the book of Hudud, Hadd is obligatory. Al-Hidayah said: and that is the most correct (al-ashah). And the commentators followed him. And in al-Tabyin: it is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) because the taking of benefit from the 'ayn is not conceivable; it is only conceivable from its monetary value, so the intercourse was not occurring in a place of taking benefit. But since the taking of benefit is a cause for ownership of wealth in a general sense, and ownership of wealth in a general sense created shubha, unlike the hired slave-girl (al-musta'jara) and the slave-girl of the deceased if the creditor has intercourse with her, because hire does not confer pleasure at all, and the creditor does not own the 'ayn of the estate; he only takes his due from its price. And if his right were attached to the 'ayn, its sale would not be permissible without his permission, like the mortgage. The summary: if he thought it was halal, then no Hadd on him by agreement of the two riwayas. The disagreement is when he knows the prohibition, and the most correct is that it is obligatory. But al-Idah mentioned a third riwaya: that Hadd is obligatory even if he says 'I thought it was halal' and his thought is not considered, by analogy with the creditor's intercourse with the slave-girl of the deceased. This riwaya contradicts the majority of riwayas, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Hidayah said: 'The borrower for the mortgage (al-musta'ir li al-rahn) is in this like the mortgagee. As for the hired slave-girl, the borrowed one, and the deposited one, they are like the slave-girl of his brother.' And it will come that Hadd is imposed even if he thought it was halal, as in al-Muhit and al-Bada'i'. And he made it general in the thought of halal, including the thought of the man and the thought of the slave-girl: if they both thought so, no Hadd; if they both knew the prohibition, Hadd is obligatory; if the man thought so and the slave-girl knew, or vice versa, no Hadd because shubha, when it is entrenched in the act on one side, necessarily extends to the other side, as in al-Muhit. The expression is the preceding one. His statement: 'and the borrower for the mortgage' means the borrower of a slave-girl for the purpose of mortgaging her, so the lam is causative. His statement: 'cause of ownership of pleasure . : the price,'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of shubha in the act by providing additional examples from the matn. It first addresses intercourse with the slave-girl of one's parents, one's wife, or one's master. Even though there is no ownership or right of ownership, customary and legal permission to benefit from wealth creates ambiguity, so if the person thought it was halal, no Hadd is imposed. The author then lists sister cases (akhawat): (1) A woman divorced in exchange for money (mukhalla'ah bi mal) – her prohibition is established by consensus, so she is like the thrice-divorced woman. However, if the khul' was done with the word 'khul', there is a difference of opinion among the Companions. Al-Karkhi states that the ruling is like the triple divorce: if the person thought it was halal due to ambiguity, no Hadd; but if he knew the prohibition, Hadd is obligatory because the removal of ownership is agreed upon. (2) Umm walad when emancipated – her prohibition is by consensus, but shubha exists due to the remaining effect of the marital bed (the iddah), so if there is ambiguity, no Hadd. (3) The mortgaged slave-girl (marhoonah) in favor of the mortgagee: there are two riwayas (transmissions) in the Hanafi school. In the riwaya of the book of Rahn, no Hadd; in the riwaya of the book of Hudud, Hadd is obligatory. Al-Hidayah prefers the latter as al-ashah (most correct), and it is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) because the mortgagee's right is only in the value, not in the 'ayn, so the act is like zina. However, if the mortgagee thought it was halal, no Hadd by agreement; the dispute is when he knows the prohibition. A third riwaya from al-Idah is mentioned but is contrary to the majority. The passage also discusses the borrower for mortgage (musta'ir li al-rahn), who is like the mortgagee. For hired, borrowed, or deposited slave-girls, Hadd is imposed unless both parties thought it was halal. If the man thinks it is halal but the slave-girl knows it is haram, or vice versa, no Hadd because shubha extends from one side to the other.

Key Terms
مرتهن (murtahin): The mortgagee (creditor) who holds a slave-girl as collateral (rahn).
مرهونة (marhoonah): A slave-girl who is given as collateral (mortgage).
مختلعة (mukhalla'ah): A woman divorced by khul' (divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation).
أمة (amah): Slave-girl; used throughout the passage for various categories.
اشتباه (ishtibah): Ambiguity or confusion that creates shubha, averting Hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-ashah) in the text, what is the ruling if a mortgagee (murtahin) has intercourse with the mortgaged slave-girl (marhoonah) knowing it is haram?
A) No Hadd because of shubha
B) Hadd is wajib
C) Ta'zir only
D) It depends on whether he thought it was halal

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that shubha in the act may avert Hadd only if the person genuinely believed the act was halal; in the case of the mortgaged slave-girl, knowledge of prohibition triggers Hadd, per the preferred view.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
Shubha in Zina: The Core Distinction Between Place and Act
Introducing the two types of shubha and their legal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubhat al-mahal and shubhat al-fi'l and state whether hadd is dropped and lineage established in each type.

Core Idea
Shubha in the place drops hadd and establishes lineage; shubha in the act drops hadd but not lineage.

The Hanafi school recognizes that the fixed punishment for zina (hadd) can be averted by a valid doubt (shubha). However, not all doubts are equal. A key distinction is between ambiguity about the person with whom intercourse occurs (shubhat al-mahal) and ambiguity about the act itself (shubhat al-fi'l). This lesson clarifies that distinction and its two major consequences: whether hadd is dropped, and whether lineage of a child is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of shubha
Locate where the text introduces 'shubhat al-mahal' and 'shubhat al-fi'l'. Note the definition of each: place-related vs. act-related doubt.
The text uses terms like 'شبهة المحل' and 'شبهة الفعل'.
2
Determine the ruling on hadd for each category
For shubhat al-mahal, the text states that hadd is dropped because the doubt is in the place itself. For shubhat al-fi'l, hadd is also dropped due to the actor's personal confusion.
Both types drop hadd, but for different reasons.
3
Examine the ruling on lineage for each category
The text explicitly says: 'والنسب يثبت في الأول فقط' – lineage is established in shubhat al-mahal by claim, but not in shubhat al-fi'l except in the specific case of the thrice-divorced woman.
Lineage is a consequence that differs between the two types.
4
Apply to a sample scenario
Take the example of a man who has intercourse with a woman sent to him as a bride (mazfoofa). This is shubhat al-mahal because the women said she is his wife, so hadd is dropped and lineage can be claimed.
Contrast with a blind man who finds a woman in his bed at night and assumes she is his wife – this is shubhat al-fi'l.

Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. Intercourse is from the right of permissibility, so zina is thereby realized. End of quote. It should be understood that the reference of the Book of Talaq is to when he divorces her with one word, and the expression of the Book of Hudud is to when he divorces her in separate [words], as we mentioned for reconciliation between them, as is not hidden. As for zina with the slave woman of his parents, his wife, and his master: he has no ownership nor right of ownership in her, but the ease [of interaction] flows among them in benefiting from wealth and consent to that customarily, and benefiting from wealth is permitted legally. So when he assumes this category, he is excused because intercourse with slave girls is of the type of using [for service], so the situation is ambiguous, and ambiguity in its place is excused therein. And these matters have sisters: among them is the irrevocably divorced woman (mubarat) for compensation, because her prohibition is established by consensus, so she becomes like a thrice-divorced woman. Thus mentioned the commentators, and they mean talaq for compensation without the word khul'. As for if it is with the word khul', we have already presented the disagreement about it and that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, differed about it. However, in al-Bada'i': If he khul'a her or divorced her for compensation and then had intercourse with her during the waiting period, al-Karkhi mentioned that the ruling should be like the ruling of the thrice-divorced woman. And it is the correct [view] because the removal of ownership by khul' and talaq for compensation is agreed upon, so the shubha did not materialize, so hadd is obligatory unless he claims ambiguity. And among them is the umm al-walad when her master frees her, because her prohibition is established by consensus, and shubha is established at the time of ambiguity due to the persistence of the effect of the bed, which is the waiting period. And among them is the mortgaged slave girl with respect to the mortgagee in one narration of the Book of Hudud. So if the mortgagee says, 'I knew she was forbidden and I had intercourse with her,' there are two narrations. In the narration of the Book of Rahn, there is no hadd upon him, and it is of the first type as we presented. In the narration of the Book of Hudud, hadd is obligatory. Al-Hidayah said: It is the most correct. And the commentators followed him. In al-Tabyin: It is the chosen [view] because satisfaction of the debt from her very self is not conceivable; rather it is only conceivable from her monetary value, so the intercourse did not occur in the place of satisfaction. But since satisfaction is a cause for ownership of money in some respect, and ownership of money in some respect occurred, ambiguity arose, unlike the hired slave girl and the slave girl of the deceased if the creditor has intercourse with her, because hiring does not impart enjoyment at all, and the creditor does not own the very estate; rather he satisfies his right from the price. And if his right was attached to the very [estate], selling it would not be permissible except with his permission, like the pawn. The conclusion: that if he assumed permissibility, there is no hadd upon him by agreement of the two narrations. The disagreement is regarding when he knew the prohibition, and the more correct [view] is its obligation. However, al-Iydah mentioned a third narration: that hadd is obligatory even if he said, 'I assumed it was permissible,' and his assumption is not considered, by analogy to the creditor's intercourse with the deceased's slave girl. And this narration contradicts most narrations, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Hidayah said: The borrower for pawn is in this like the mortgagee. As for the hired slave girl, the borrowed one, and the deposit, they are like the slave girl of his brother. And it will come that he will be punished by hadd even if he assumed permissibility, as in al-Muhit and al-Bada'i. And he spoke generally about the assumption of permissibility, so it includes the man's assumption and the slave girl's assumption. If both assume, no hadd; if both know the prohibition, hadd is obligatory; if the man assumes and the slave girl knows, or vice versa, no hadd because the shubha, when it takes root in the act in one side, extends to the other side necessarily. Thus in al-Muhit. The expression is the preceding. His statement: (and the borrower for pawn) i.e., the one who borrows a slave girl in order to pawn her, so the lam is for cause. His statement: (cause for ownership of enjoyment). The price. The Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. In the first only, and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed, not an alien woman who is sent [to him] and it is said she is your wife, and upon him is mahr, and with a mahram he married, and for an alien woman in other than the vagina. His statement: (and lineage is established in the first only) i.e., lineage is established in shubha of place (shubhat al-mahal) by claim, and is not established in shubha of act (shubhat al-fi'l) even if he claims it, because the act is pure zina in the second even though hadd is dropped for a reason pertaining to him, which is the ambiguity upon him, and it is not pure in the first due to the shubha in the place. And the author has already presented that the lineage of the child of the irrevocably divorced woman is established if she gives birth within less than two years without claim, and for two years or more it is not established except by claim, and its generality includes the divorced woman of three talags, so it was a specification of his statement here: 'only'. The conclusion: that lineage is not established in shubha of act upon claim except in the thrice-divorced woman. The difference is that the shubha in her is a shubha in the contract, unlike the rest of the places of shubha of ambiguity, because there is no shubha of contract in them, so lineage is not established by claim. And it will come that among shubha of ambiguity is the intercourse with a woman who is sent [to a man] and the women say she is your wife while she is not his wife, relying on their news. Al-Zayla'i explicitly stated that lineage is established therein by claim as will come. So it is settled that lineage is not established in shubha of act except in two places. His statement: (and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed) meaning whether he assumed permissibility or prohibition, because there is no ease [of interaction] with the brother and uncle, and likewise the rest of the mahram except for parents, as we explained, and there is no ambiguity in the woman found on his bed due to long companionship, so it is not based on evidence. And this is because someone else from the mahram who are in her house may sleep on his bed. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind because he can distinguish by asking and otherwise, except if he called her and she responded and said, 'I am your wife,' or 'I am so-and-so by the name of his wife,' and he had intercourse with her, because the information is evidence. In al-Tabyin: And if she gives birth to a child, his lineage is established for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa' (likely 'al-mazfoofa'), and if she only responded, he is punished by hadd due to lack of [evidence] for dropping [hadd]. And he spoke generally about the woman, so it includes the coerced and the willing; if he coerced her, he is punished and she is not, and mahr is not obligatory according to us. His statement: (not an alien woman who is sent and it is said she is your wife) i.e., he is not punished by hadd for intercourse with an alien woman who is sent to him and the women say [she is your wife]. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind. It is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya what is here from al-Muntaqa and al-Asl, then he said al-Khulasa: If a blind man found in his bed or room a woman and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife,' Abu Yusuf said: He is not excused. Zufar said: Hadd is averted from him and he owes the dower (al-uqr). Al-Zahiriyya: A man found in his house a woman on a dark night and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – no hadd upon him; if it was daytime, he is punished. Al-Hawi: From Zufar from Abu Hanifa regarding one who found in his private room or house a woman and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – if it was daytime, he is punished; if it was night, no hadd. From Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa: Upon him is hadd, night or day. Abu al-Layth al-Kabir said: And by the narration of Zufar, it is taken (i.e., acted upon). I say: The implication of this is that there is no hadd on the blind person, night or day. Reflect. His statement: (for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa') – thus in the copies with two qafs after the ra, and the correct is 'al-mazfoofa' with a zaal and two fā's, i.e., the matter of the alien woman who is sent to him, coming after this. Then it is not hidden that the intended meaning of his statement 'and if she gives birth to a child etc.' is when he called her and she responded, because lineage is not established except with verification of permissibility from one aspect. As for when there is no shubha at all, it is not established.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqaiq about intercourse that obligates the hadd penalty and that which does not. The author discusses various scenarios where a shubha (doubt/ambiguity) exists that may prevent the hadd from being applied. The key distinction is between shubha in the place (shubhat al-mahal), where there is some basis for believing the intercourse is permissible (e.g., a slave woman shared by family, or a woman in one's bed due to some common usage), and shubha in the act (shubhat al-fi'l), where the act is clearly zina but hadd is dropped due to a personal doubt (like a blind man claiming he thought she was his wife). In shubha of place, lineage may be established by claim; in shubha of act, lineage is not established except in the specific case of the thrice-divorced woman during her iddah. The text also details intra-Hanafi differences, such as between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding the blind man or night-time scenarios. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if the person thought it was permissible due to some valid reason (like ease of interaction with family slave women), hadd is dropped; if he knew it was haram, hadd is obligatory, except in cases of clear shubha. The author also explains that if both parties (man and woman) assume permissibility, no hadd; if one knows it is haram, the shubha extends to both and no hadd. This is based on the principle of necessity and avoidance of the penalty in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
شبهة المحل (shubhat al-mahal): Ambiguity concerning the place or the person with whom intercourse occurs, such as a woman who might be permissible due to some right or relationship.
شبهة الفعل (shubhat al-fi'l): Ambiguity concerning the act itself, where the person is unaware it is unlawful due to lack of knowledge, such as a blind man thinking a woman is his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, lineage is established by claim in which type of shubha?
A) Shubhat al-fi'l only
B) Shubhat al-mahal only
C) Both types equally
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
Shubhat al-mahal drops hadd and establishes lineage; shubhat al-fi'l drops hadd but does not establish lineage except in the specific case of the thrice-divorced woman in her iddah.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

When Shubha Drops Hadd: Specific Scenarios and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Detailed application of shubha rules to specific disputed cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of shubha to specific scenarios like the mortgaged slave girl and the blind man, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Shubha can arise from customary ease, a beneficial interest, or factual uncertainty; the relied-upon view maximizes avoidance of hadd.

The previous lesson laid out the two broad categories of shubha. Now we turn to the specific cases that the Hanafi jurists debated: the mortgaged slave girl, the blind man finding a woman in his bed, the woman sent as a bride, and others. These scenarios reveal important intra-Hanafi differences, with the later school (mufta bihi) tending to give the benefit of the doubt to the accused wherever possible, consistent with the maxim 'أدرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' (avert hadd by doubts).

Guided Path
1
Examine the case of the mortgaged slave girl
Find the discussion of the mortgagee who has intercourse with the mortgaged slave girl. Note the two narrations: from Kitab al-Rahn (no hadd) and Kitab al-Hudud (hadd). Understand the reasoning: is there a shubha based on a right of satisfaction?
Al-Hidayah sides with Kitab al-Hudud – hadd is obligatory.
2
Analyze the blind man scenario
Track the different positions: Abu Yusuf (hadd), Zufar (no hadd), Abu Hanifa differentiated day/night via Zufar, Ya'qub (always hadd). Note the tarjih by Abu al-Layth that Zufar's narration is taken.
The relied-upon position drops hadd for the blind man even in daytime, due to his condition.
3
Consider the woman sent as a bride (mazfoofa)
The text states that if an alien woman is sent to a man and women say she is his wife, he is not punished for intercourse. This is shubhat al-mahal based on the women's testimony.
This also establishes lineage, as will be seen in the next passage.
4
Identify the common principle
In each case, the shubha arises from a plausible basis: a right, a custom, or a report. If the person actually knew it was haram, hadd is imposed unless the shubha extends to both parties.
The text says if one of them knows and the other assumes, hadd is dropped because shubha extends.

أطلقه فشمل البصير والأعمى لأنه يمكنه التمييز بالسؤال وغيره إلا إذا دعاها فأجابته وقالت أنا زوجتك أو أنا فلانة باسم زوجته فواقعها لأن الإخبار دليل. وفي التبيين : وإن جاءت بولد يثبت نسبه لما نذكره في Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. Intercourse is from the right of permissibility, so zina is thereby realized. End of quote. It should be understood that the reference of the Book of Talaq is to when he divorces her with one word, and the expression of the Book of Hudud is to when he divorces her in separate [words], as we mentioned for reconciliation between them, as is not hidden. As for zina with the slave woman of his parents, his wife, and his master: he has no ownership nor right of ownership in her, but the ease [of interaction] flows among them in benefiting from wealth and consent to that customarily, and benefiting from wealth is permitted legally. So when he assumes this category, he is excused because intercourse with slave girls is of the type of using [for service], so the situation is ambiguous, and ambiguity in its place is excused therein. And these matters have sisters: among them is the irrevocably divorced woman (mubarat) for compensation, because her prohibition is established by consensus, so she becomes like a thrice-divorced woman. Thus mentioned the commentators, and they mean talaq for compensation without the word khul'. As for if it is with the word khul', we have already presented the disagreement about it and that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, differed about it. However, in al-Bada'i': If he khul'a her or divorced her for compensation and then had intercourse with her during the waiting period, al-Karkhi mentioned that the ruling should be like the ruling of the thrice-divorced woman. And it is the correct [view] because the removal of ownership by khul' and talaq for compensation is agreed upon, so the shubha did not materialize, so hadd is obligatory unless he claims ambiguity. And among them is the umm al-walad when her master frees her, because her prohibition is established by consensus, and shubha is established at the time of ambiguity due to the persistence of the effect of the bed, which is the waiting period. And among them is the mortgaged slave girl with respect to the mortgagee in one narration of the Book of Hudud. So if the mortgagee says, 'I knew she was forbidden and I had intercourse with her,' there are two narrations. In the narration of the Book of Rahn, there is no hadd upon him, and it is of the first type as we presented. In the narration of the Book of Hudud, hadd is obligatory. Al-Hidayah said: It is the most correct. And the commentators followed him. In al-Tabyin: It is the chosen [view] because satisfaction of the debt from her very self is not conceivable; rather it is only conceivable from her monetary value, so the intercourse did not occur in the place of satisfaction. But since satisfaction is a cause for ownership of money in some respect, and ownership of money in some respect occurred, ambiguity arose, unlike the hired slave girl and the slave girl of the deceased if the creditor has intercourse with her, because hiring does not impart enjoyment at all, and the creditor does not own the very estate; rather he satisfies his right from the price. And if his right was attached to the very [estate], selling it would not be permissible except with his permission, like the pawn. The conclusion: that if he assumed permissibility, there is no hadd upon him by agreement of the two narrations. The disagreement is regarding when he knew the prohibition, and the more correct [view] is its obligation. However, al-Iydah mentioned a third narration: that hadd is obligatory even if he said, 'I assumed it was permissible,' and his assumption is not considered, by analogy to the creditor's intercourse with the deceased's slave girl. And this narration contradicts most narrations, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Hidayah said: The borrower for pawn is in this like the mortgagee. As for the hired slave girl, the borrowed one, and the deposit, they are like the slave girl of his brother. And it will come that he will be punished by hadd even if he assumed permissibility, as in al-Muhit and al-Bada'i. And he spoke generally about the assumption of permissibility, so it includes the man's assumption and the slave girl's assumption. If both assume, no hadd; if both know the prohibition, hadd is obligatory; if the man assumes and the slave girl knows, or vice versa, no hadd because the shubha, when it takes root in the act in one side, extends to the other side necessarily. Thus in al-Muhit. The expression is the preceding. His statement: (and the borrower for pawn) i.e., the one who borrows a slave girl in order to pawn her, so the lam is for cause. His statement: (cause for ownership of enjoyment). The price. The Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. In the first only, and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed, not an alien woman who is sent [to him] and it is said she is your wife, and upon him is mahr, and with a mahram he married, and for an alien woman in other than the vagina. His statement: (and lineage is established in the first only) i.e., lineage is established in shubha of place (shubhat al-mahal) by claim, and is not established in shubha of act (shubhat al-fi'l) even if he claims it, because the act is pure zina in the second even though hadd is dropped for a reason pertaining to him, which is the ambiguity upon him, and it is not pure in the first due to the shubha in the place. And the author has already presented that the lineage of the child of the irrevocably divorced woman is established if she gives birth within less than two years without claim, and for two years or more it is not established except by claim, and its generality includes the divorced woman of three talags, so it was a specification of his statement here: 'only'. The conclusion: that lineage is not established in shubha of act upon claim except in the thrice-divorced woman. The difference is that the shubha in her is a shubha in the contract, unlike the rest of the places of shubha of ambiguity, because there is no shubha of contract in them, so lineage is not established by claim. And it will come that among shubha of ambiguity is the intercourse with a woman who is sent [to a man] and the women say she is your wife while she is not his wife, relying on their news. Al-Zayla'i explicitly stated that lineage is established therein by claim as will come. So it is settled that lineage is not established in shubha of act except in two places. His statement: (and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed) meaning whether he assumed permissibility or prohibition, because there is no ease [of interaction] with the brother and uncle, and likewise the rest of the mahram except for parents, as we explained, and there is no ambiguity in the woman found on his bed due to long companionship, so it is not based on evidence. And this is because someone else from the mahram who are in her house may sleep on his bed. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind because he can distinguish by asking and otherwise, except if he called her and she responded and said, 'I am your wife,' or 'I am so-and-so by the name of his wife,' and he had intercourse with her, because the information is evidence. In al-Tabyin: And if she gives birth to a child, his lineage is established for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa' (likely 'al-mazfoofa'), and if she only responded, he is punished by hadd due to lack of [evidence] for dropping [hadd]. And he spoke generally about the woman, so it includes the coerced and the willing; if he coerced her, he is punished and she is not, and mahr is not obligatory according to us. His statement: (not an alien woman who is sent and it is said she is your wife) i.e., he is not punished by hadd for intercourse with an alien woman who is sent to him and the women say [she is your wife]. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind. It is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya what is here from al-Muntaqa and al-Asl, then he said al-Khulasa: If a blind man found in his bed or room a woman and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife,' Abu Yusuf said: He is not excused. Zufar said: Hadd is averted from him and he owes the dower (al-uqr). Al-Zahiriyya: A man found in his house a woman on a dark night and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – no hadd upon him; if it was daytime, he is punished. Al-Hawi: From Zufar from Abu Hanifa regarding one who found in his private room or house a woman and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – if it was daytime, he is punished; if it was night, no hadd. From Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa: Upon him is hadd, night or day. Abu al-Layth al-Kabir said: And by the narration of Zufar, it is taken (i.e., acted upon). I say: The implication of this is that there is no hadd on the blind person, night or day. Reflect. His statement: (for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa') – thus in the copies with two qafs after the ra, and the correct is 'al-mazfoofa' with a zaal and two fā's, i.e., the matter of the alien woman who is sent to him, coming after this. Then it is not hidden that the intended meaning of his statement 'and if she gives birth to a child etc.' is when he called her and she responded, because lineage is not established except with verification of permissibility from one aspect. As for when there is no shubha at all, it is not established.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqaiq about intercourse that obligates the hadd penalty and that which does not. The author discusses various scenarios where a shubha (doubt/ambiguity) exists that may prevent the hadd from being applied. The key distinction is between shubha in the place (shubhat al-mahal), where there is some basis for believing the intercourse is permissible (e.g., a slave woman shared by family, or a woman in one's bed due to some common usage), and shubha in the act (shubhat al-fi'l), where the act is clearly zina but hadd is dropped due to a personal doubt (like a blind man claiming he thought she was his wife). In shubha of place, lineage may be established by claim; in shubha of act, lineage is not established except in the specific case of the thrice-divorced woman during her iddah. The text also details intra-Hanafi differences, such as between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding the blind man or night-time scenarios. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if the person thought it was permissible due to some valid reason (like ease of interaction with family slave women), hadd is dropped; if he knew it was haram, hadd is obligatory, except in cases of clear shubha. The author also explains that if both parties (man and woman) assume permissibility, no hadd; if one knows it is haram, the shubha extends to both and no hadd. This is based on the principle of necessity and avoidance of the penalty in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
مرقوقة/مزفوفة (marqooqa/mazfoofa): A woman who is sent to a man (usually for marriage) by her family, leading the man to believe she is his wife.
عقر (uqr): Compensation paid by a man who has intercourse with a woman under a shubha; essentially the dower (mahr al-mithl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the text, what is the ruling for a mortgagee who has intercourse with a mortgaged slave girl knowing it is haram?
A) No hadd, because he has a right to her use.
B) Hadd is obligatory, because his right is only to the monetary value.
C) Hadd is discretionary.
D) The matter is left to the judge's opinion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has multiple narrations on many shubha scenarios; the mufta bihi tends to favour avoiding hadd where there is a plausible doubt, as seen in the blind man case, while for the mortgagee, the stricter view prevails.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
When Hadd is Not Dropped: Cases of Baseless Claims and the Principle of No Shubha
Clarifying when a claim of permissibility is insufficient to avert hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify scenarios where the Hanafi school imposes hadd despite a claim of doubt, and explain why no shubha exists.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a brother's slave girl or a woman found in one's bed is not excused by a mere claim of ignorance because there is no customary or evidentiary basis for doubt.

Not every claim of ignorance or doubt drops the hadd. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between genuine shubha and baseless excuses. The text explicitly states that a man who has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave girl is punished, even if he claims he thought it was permissible. Similarly, a woman found in one's bed (under normal circumstances) does not create a shubha because long companionship does not make it ambiguous. This lesson examines these non-shubha cases and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases where hadd is applied despite a claim of permissibility
The text lists: 'بحال' with brother's/uncle's slave girl, and the woman found in his bed. These are mentioned at the beginning: 'وحد بوطء أمة أخيه وعمه وإن ظن حله وامرأة وجدت في فراشه'.
Note the phrase 'وإن ظن حله' – even if he thought it was permissible.
2
Understand why no shubha exists in these cases
For the brother's slave girl, there is no customary ease (انبساط) between brothers over each other's property. For the woman found in bed, there is no ambiguity because he knows his own bed and who normally sleeps there.
The text says: 'لأنه لا انبساط في الأخ والعم وكذا سائر المحارم' and 'ولا اشتباه في المرأة الموجودة على فراشه لطول الصحبة'.
3
Examine the exception for the blind man who calls and the woman responds
If the man calls out and the woman responds claiming to be his wife, then hadd is dropped because her response provides a plausible basis for doubt. But if she merely responds without that claim, hadd remains.
The text distinguishes between a responsive claim and a mere response.
4
Apply the rule to other prohibited relationships
The text analogizes 'سائر المحارم' (all other mahram relatives) to the brother/uncle case: no shubha, hadd is applied. The exception is only for the direct ascendants (parents) where customary ease may exist.
Remember the text says 'سوى الولاد' – except for parents, where shubha might exist due to shared household.

كتاب الحدود باب الوطء الذي يوجب الحد والذي لا يوجبه الوطء من حق الحل فيتحقق الزنا .اهـ. وينبغي أن تحمل إشارة كتاب الطلاق على ما إذا أوقعها بكلمة واحدة، وعبارة كتاب الحدود على ما إذا أوقعها متفرقة لما ذكرنا توفيقاً بينهما كما لا يخفى . وأما الزنا بأمة أبويه وزوجته وسيده فإنه لا ملك له ولا حق ملك فيها غير أن البسوطة تجري بينهم في الانتفاع بالأموال والرضا بذلك عادة وهي تجوز الانتفاع بالمال شرعاً، فإذا ظن هذا القبيل يعذر لأن وطء الجواري من قبيل الاستخدام فيشتبه الحال والاشتباه في محله معذور فيه. ولهذه المسائل أخوات منها : المطلقة على مال لأن حرمتها ثابتة بالإجماع فصارت كالمطلقة ثلاثاً ؛ كذا ذكره الشارحون ومرادهم الطلاق على مال بغير لفظ الخلع، أما إذا كان بلفظ الخلع فقد قدمنا الاختلاف فيه وأن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم اختلفوا فيه لكن في البدائع : ولو خالعها أو طلقها على مال فوطئها في العدة ذكر الكرخي أنه ينبغي أن يكون الحكم فيه كالحكم في المطلقة ثلاثاً . وهو الصحيح لأن زوال الملك بالخلع والطلاق على مال مجمع عليه فلم تتحقق الشبهة فيجب الحد إلا إذا ادعى الاشتباه ومنها أم الولد إذا أعتقها مولاها لثبوت حرمتها بالإجماع وتثبت الشبهة عند الاشتباه لبقاء أثر الفراش وهي العدة . ومنها الجارية المرهونة في حق المرتهن في رواية كتاب الحدود فإذا قال المرتهن علمت أنها حرام ووطئتها ففيه روايتان، ففي رواية كتاب الرهن لا حد عليه وهو من النوع الأول لما قدمناه ، وفي وراية كتاب الحدود يجب الحد قال في الهداية وهو الأصح. وتبعه الشارحون. وفي التبيين : وهو المختار لأن الاستيفاء من عينها لا يتصور وإنما يتصور من ماليتها فلم يكن الوطء حاصلاً في محل الاستيفاء لكن لما كان الاستيفاء سبباً لملك المال في الجملة وملك المال في الجملة حصل الاشتباه بخلاف المستأجرة وجارية الميت إذا وطئها الغريم لأن الإجارة لا تفيد المتعة بحال والغريم لا يملك عين التركة وإنما يستوفي حقه من ولو تعلق حقه بالعين لما جاز بيعها إلا بإذنه كالرهن والحاصل أنه إذا ظن الحل فلا حد عليه باتفاق الروايتين، والخلاف فيما إذا علم الحرمة والأصح وجوبه لكن ذكر في الإيضاح رواية ثالثة أنه يجب الحد وإن قال ظننت أنها حلال وإن ظنه لا يعتبر قياساً على وطء الغريم جارية الميت، وهذه الرواية مخالفة لعامة الروايات كما في فتح القدير : قال في الهداية : والمستعير للرهن في هذا بمنزلة المرتهن وأما الجارية المستأجرة والعارية والوديعة فكجارية أخيه، وسيأتي أنه يحد وإن ظن الحل كما في المحيط والبدائع وأطلق في ظن الحل فشمل ظن الرجل وظن الجارية فإن ظناه فلا حد وإن علما الحرمة وجب الحد، وإن ظنه الرجل وعلمته الجارية أو بالعكس فلا حد لأن الشبهة إذا تمكنت في الفعل في أحد الجانبين تتعدى إلى الجانب الآخر ضرورة؛ كذا في المحيط . العبارة هي المتقدمة. قوله : ( والمستعير للرهن أي المستعير أمة لأجل أن يرهنها فاللام تعليلية. قوله : سبب لملك المتعة . : الثمن، كتاب الحدود باب الوطء الذي يوجب الحد والذي لا يوجبه في الأول فقط وحد ٢٣ بوطء أمة أخيه وعمه وإن ظن حله وامرأة وجدت في فراشه لا بأجنبيه ؛ زفت وقيل هي زوجتك وعليه مهر وبمحرم نكحها وفي أجنبية في غير قبل قوله : (والنسب يثبت في الأول فقط) أي يثبت النسب في شبهة المحل بالدعوة ولا يثبت في شبهة الفعل وإن ادعاه لأن الفعل تمحض زنا في الثانية وإن سقط الحد لأمر راجع إليه وهو اشتباه الأمر عليه ولم يتمحض في الأولى للشبهة في المحل، وقد قدم المصنف أن نسب ولد المعتدة البت يثبت إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين بغير دعوة، ولسنتين فأكثر لا يثبت إلا بالدعوة، وهو بعمومه يتناول المعتدة عن ثلاث طلقات فكان مخصصاً لقوله هنا .«فقط». والحاصل أنه لا يثبت النسب في شبهة الفعل عند الدعوة إلا في المطلقة ثلاثاً. والفرق أن الشبهة فيها شبهة في العقد بخلاف باقي محال شبهة الاشتباه فإنه لا شبهة عقد فيها فلا يثبت النسب بالدعوة وسيأتي أن من شبهة الاشتباه وطء امرأة زفت وقالت النساء هي زوجتك ولم تكن زوجته معتمداً خبرهن. وصرح الزيلعي بأن النسب يثبت فيه بالدعوة كما سيأتي فتحرر أن النسب لا يثبت في شبهة الفعل إلا في موضعين قوله : (وحد بوطء أمة أخيه وعمه وإن ظن حله وامرأة وجدت في فراشه) يعني سواء ظن الحل أو الحرمة لأنه لا انبساط في الأخ والعم وكذا سائر المحارم سوى الولاد لما بينا ولا اشتباه في المرأة الموجودة على فراشه لطول الصحبة فلم يكن مستنداً إلى دليل، وهذا لأنه قد ينام على فراشه غيرها من المحارم التي في بيتها . أطلقه فشمل البصير والأعمى لأنه يمكنه التمييز بالسؤال وغيره إلا إذا دعاها فأجابته وقالت أنا زوجتك أو أنا فلانة باسم زوجته فواقعها لأن الإخبار دليل. وفي التبيين : وإن جاءت بولد يثبت نسبه لما نذكره في المرقوقة ولو أجابته فقط يحد لعدم ما السقوط. وأطلق في المرأة فشمل المكرهة والطائعة فيحد لو أكرهها دونها ولا يجب المهر عندنا قوله : (لا بأجنبية زفت وقيل هي زوجتك) أي لا يحد بوطء أجنبية زفت إليه وقال النساء : مال يوجب هي أطلقه فشمل البصير والأعمى الخ) نقل في التتارخانية ما هنا عن المنتقى والأصل ثم قال الخلاصة : ولو أن أعمى وجد في فراشه أو حجرته امرأة فوقع عليها وقال ظننت أنها امرأتي قال أبو يوسف : لا يعذر. وقال زفر يدرأ عنه الحد وعليه العقر الظهيرية : رجل وجد في بيته امرأة في ليلة ظلماء فغشيها وقال ظننت أنها امرأتي لا حد عليه ولو كان نهاراً يحد الحاوي وعن زفر عن أبي حنيفة فيمن وجد في حجلته أو بيته امرأة فقال ظننت أنها امرأتي إن كان نهاراً ,يحد، وإن كان ليلاً لا يحد. وعن يعقوب عن أبي حنيفة أن عليه الحد ليلاً كان أو نهاراً. قال أبو الليث الكبير : وبرواية زفر يؤخذ اهـ . قلت : ومقتضى هذا أنه لا حد على الأعمى ليلاً كان أو نهاراً تأمل. قوله : (لما نذكره في المرقوقة) كذا في النسخ بقافين بعد الراء والصواب المزفوفة بالزاي المعجمة وفاءين أي في مسألة الأجنبية التي زفت إليه الآتية تلو .هذه ثم لا يخفى أن المراد بقوله وإن جاءت بولد إلى آخره» ما إذا دعاها فأجابته لأن النسب لا يثبت إلا بتحقق الحل من وجه أما عند عدم الشبهة أصلاً فلا يثبت
Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. Intercourse is from the right of permissibility, so zina is thereby realized. End of quote. It should be understood that the reference of the Book of Talaq is to when he divorces her with one word, and the expression of the Book of Hudud is to when he divorces her in separate [words], as we mentioned for reconciliation between them, as is not hidden. As for zina with the slave woman of his parents, his wife, and his master: he has no ownership nor right of ownership in her, but the ease [of interaction] flows among them in benefiting from wealth and consent to that customarily, and benefiting from wealth is permitted legally. So when he assumes this category, he is excused because intercourse with slave girls is of the type of using [for service], so the situation is ambiguous, and ambiguity in its place is excused therein. And these matters have sisters: among them is the irrevocably divorced woman (mubarat) for compensation, because her prohibition is established by consensus, so she becomes like a thrice-divorced woman. Thus mentioned the commentators, and they mean talaq for compensation without the word khul'. As for if it is with the word khul', we have already presented the disagreement about it and that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, differed about it. However, in al-Bada'i': If he khul'a her or divorced her for compensation and then had intercourse with her during the waiting period, al-Karkhi mentioned that the ruling should be like the ruling of the thrice-divorced woman. And it is the correct [view] because the removal of ownership by khul' and talaq for compensation is agreed upon, so the shubha did not materialize, so hadd is obligatory unless he claims ambiguity. And among them is the umm al-walad when her master frees her, because her prohibition is established by consensus, and shubha is established at the time of ambiguity due to the persistence of the effect of the bed, which is the waiting period. And among them is the mortgaged slave girl with respect to the mortgagee in one narration of the Book of Hudud. So if the mortgagee says, 'I knew she was forbidden and I had intercourse with her,' there are two narrations. In the narration of the Book of Rahn, there is no hadd upon him, and it is of the first type as we presented. In the narration of the Book of Hudud, hadd is obligatory. Al-Hidayah said: It is the most correct. And the commentators followed him. In al-Tabyin: It is the chosen [view] because satisfaction of the debt from her very self is not conceivable; rather it is only conceivable from her monetary value, so the intercourse did not occur in the place of satisfaction. But since satisfaction is a cause for ownership of money in some respect, and ownership of money in some respect occurred, ambiguity arose, unlike the hired slave girl and the slave girl of the deceased if the creditor has intercourse with her, because hiring does not impart enjoyment at all, and the creditor does not own the very estate; rather he satisfies his right from the price. And if his right was attached to the very [estate], selling it would not be permissible except with his permission, like the pawn. The conclusion: that if he assumed permissibility, there is no hadd upon him by agreement of the two narrations. The disagreement is regarding when he knew the prohibition, and the more correct [view] is its obligation. However, al-Iydah mentioned a third narration: that hadd is obligatory even if he said, 'I assumed it was permissible,' and his assumption is not considered, by analogy to the creditor's intercourse with the deceased's slave girl. And this narration contradicts most narrations, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Hidayah said: The borrower for pawn is in this like the mortgagee. As for the hired slave girl, the borrowed one, and the deposit, they are like the slave girl of his brother. And it will come that he will be punished by hadd even if he assumed permissibility, as in al-Muhit and al-Bada'i. And he spoke generally about the assumption of permissibility, so it includes the man's assumption and the slave girl's assumption. If both assume, no hadd; if both know the prohibition, hadd is obligatory; if the man assumes and the slave girl knows, or vice versa, no hadd because the shubha, when it takes root in the act in one side, extends to the other side necessarily. Thus in al-Muhit. The expression is the preceding. His statement: (and the borrower for pawn) i.e., the one who borrows a slave girl in order to pawn her, so the lam is for cause. His statement: (cause for ownership of enjoyment). The price. The Book of Hudud, Chapter on Intercourse that Obligates Hadd and That Which Does Not. In the first only, and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed, not an alien woman who is sent [to him] and it is said she is your wife, and upon him is mahr, and with a mahram he married, and for an alien woman in other than the vagina. His statement: (and lineage is established in the first only) i.e., lineage is established in shubha of place (shubhat al-mahal) by claim, and is not established in shubha of act (shubhat al-fi'l) even if he claims it, because the act is pure zina in the second even though hadd is dropped for a reason pertaining to him, which is the ambiguity upon him, and it is not pure in the first due to the shubha in the place. And the author has already presented that the lineage of the child of the irrevocably divorced woman is established if she gives birth within less than two years without claim, and for two years or more it is not established except by claim, and its generality includes the divorced woman of three talags, so it was a specification of his statement here: 'only'. The conclusion: that lineage is not established in shubha of act upon claim except in the thrice-divorced woman. The difference is that the shubha in her is a shubha in the contract, unlike the rest of the places of shubha of ambiguity, because there is no shubha of contract in them, so lineage is not established by claim. And it will come that among shubha of ambiguity is the intercourse with a woman who is sent [to a man] and the women say she is your wife while she is not his wife, relying on their news. Al-Zayla'i explicitly stated that lineage is established therein by claim as will come. So it is settled that lineage is not established in shubha of act except in two places. His statement: (and he is punished by hadd for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother and his paternal uncle even if he assumed its permissibility, and the woman found in his bed) meaning whether he assumed permissibility or prohibition, because there is no ease [of interaction] with the brother and uncle, and likewise the rest of the mahram except for parents, as we explained, and there is no ambiguity in the woman found on his bed due to long companionship, so it is not based on evidence. And this is because someone else from the mahram who are in her house may sleep on his bed. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind because he can distinguish by asking and otherwise, except if he called her and she responded and said, 'I am your wife,' or 'I am so-and-so by the name of his wife,' and he had intercourse with her, because the information is evidence. In al-Tabyin: And if she gives birth to a child, his lineage is established for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa' (likely 'al-mazfoofa'), and if she only responded, he is punished by hadd due to lack of [evidence] for dropping [hadd]. And he spoke generally about the woman, so it includes the coerced and the willing; if he coerced her, he is punished and she is not, and mahr is not obligatory according to us. His statement: (not an alien woman who is sent and it is said she is your wife) i.e., he is not punished by hadd for intercourse with an alien woman who is sent to him and the women say [she is your wife]. He spoke generally, so it includes the sighted and the blind. It is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya what is here from al-Muntaqa and al-Asl, then he said al-Khulasa: If a blind man found in his bed or room a woman and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife,' Abu Yusuf said: He is not excused. Zufar said: Hadd is averted from him and he owes the dower (al-uqr). Al-Zahiriyya: A man found in his house a woman on a dark night and had intercourse with her and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – no hadd upon him; if it was daytime, he is punished. Al-Hawi: From Zufar from Abu Hanifa regarding one who found in his private room or house a woman and said, 'I assumed she was my wife' – if it was daytime, he is punished; if it was night, no hadd. From Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa: Upon him is hadd, night or day. Abu al-Layth al-Kabir said: And by the narration of Zufar, it is taken (i.e., acted upon). I say: The implication of this is that there is no hadd on the blind person, night or day. Reflect. His statement: (for what we will mention in 'al-marqooqa') – thus in the copies with two qafs after the ra, and the correct is 'al-mazfoofa' with a zaal and two fā's, i.e., the matter of the alien woman who is sent to him, coming after this. Then it is not hidden that the intended meaning of his statement 'and if she gives birth to a child etc.' is when he called her and she responded, because lineage is not established except with verification of permissibility from one aspect. As for when there is no shubha at all, it is not established.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqaiq about intercourse that obligates the hadd penalty and that which does not. The author discusses various scenarios where a shubha (doubt/ambiguity) exists that may prevent the hadd from being applied. The key distinction is between shubha in the place (shubhat al-mahal), where there is some basis for believing the intercourse is permissible (e.g., a slave woman shared by family, or a woman in one's bed due to some common usage), and shubha in the act (shubhat al-fi'l), where the act is clearly zina but hadd is dropped due to a personal doubt (like a blind man claiming he thought she was his wife). In shubha of place, lineage may be established by claim; in shubha of act, lineage is not established except in the specific case of the thrice-divorced woman during her iddah. The text also details intra-Hanafi differences, such as between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding the blind man or night-time scenarios. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if the person thought it was permissible due to some valid reason (like ease of interaction with family slave women), hadd is dropped; if he knew it was haram, hadd is obligatory, except in cases of clear shubha. The author also explains that if both parties (man and woman) assume permissibility, no hadd; if one knows it is haram, the shubha extends to both and no hadd. This is based on the principle of necessity and avoidance of the penalty in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
انبساط (inbisat): Customary ease or familiarity that allows one to benefit from another's property, used here to determine whether a shubha exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is a man punished for intercourse with the slave girl of his brother, even if he thought it was permissible?
A) Because his brother's slave girl is mahram to him.
B) Because there is no customary ease (inbisat) between brothers over such matters.
C) Because the slave girl herself must consent.
D) Because the brother's permission is always required.

Key Takeaway
Hadd is only dropped when there is a genuine shubha based on custom, testimony, or practical difficulty; baseless claims or mere presence without evidence do not avert the penalty.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
Hadd for Intercourse with Specific Women and the Role of Shubha
Explanation of hadd conditions and the shubha exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which hadd is imposed for intercourse with certain women and identify when a credible report suffices to drop the hadd.

Core Idea
Hadd applies unless the man relied on a credible report in a situation of genuine confusion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when the fixed punishment (hadd) for unlawful intercourse is imposed on a man who has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave-girl, or with a woman found in his bed. It clarifies that mere ignorance of the prohibition does not prevent hadd, but a report that the woman is his wife – if relied upon – creates a valid shubha (doubt) that drops the punishment. It also addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement about the number of women whose report suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The passage first states that hadd is imposed for intercourse with a brother's/uncle's slave-girl or a woman found in the man's bed, regardless of whether he thought it was halal. Determnine the default ruling for these cases.
The reason given is that there is no scope for 'expansion' (inbisat) with these women, and no confusion about a woman found in one's habitual sleeping place.
2
Recognize the exception
If the man calls the woman and she responds by identifying herself as his wife (or by his wife's name), then hadd is dropped. Identify the condition that creates a valid shubha.
The man's reliance on her report is considered evidence (dalil) in a situation where he cannot immediately distinguish the woman.
3
Understand the basis for dropping hadd
The commentator cites the precedent of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) and argues that the man is like a deceived person (maghrur). Trace the legal reasoning: the report is evidence, so it generates shubha.
The report is treated as evidence in a place of confusion; hence the hadd is averted.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Quduri's matn uses the plural 'women' (qulna al-nisa'), implying more than one woman is needed. The author of al-Hidayah holds that one woman suffices. Compare the two positions and see how the commentator resolves it.
The dispute hinges on whether the matter is transactional (mu'amala), where one witness suffices.
5
Apply the rule to related scenarios
Consider the case where the woman merely responds without claiming to be the wife, or where she is coerced. Determine whether hadd still applies.
The text explicitly states that in these scenarios hadd remains, confirming that the report must be explicit.
