No Hadd for Bestiality: The Hanafi Position
Ruling on bestiality: no hadd, only ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that bestiality does not incur hadd and explain why using the school's reasoning.

Core Idea
Bestiality brings ta'zir, not hadd, because it is not analogous to zina.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that bestiality is not subject to the fixed punishment (hadd) of zina. The author explains the reasoning: the act is not considered a crime against lineage, and sound nature recoils from it. Only a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) is applied. Additionally, the passage mentions the tradition of slaughtering and burning the animal, but states it is not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the primary ruling
The act is intercourse with an animal (wat' al-bahīmah). The primary ruling is that no hadd punishment is imposed.
Hadd is a fixed punishment prescribed by Shariah, e.g., 100 lashes for zina.
2
Analyze the reasoning (ta‘līl)
The text gives two reasons: (1) the act is not analogous to zina in being an offense (jīnāyah) violating lineage, and (2) sound nature (tab‘ salīm) is repulsed by it; only extreme foolishness or excessive lust drives it.
The principle is that qiyas requires complete analogy; here the presence of motive is different.
3
State the consequence: ta‘zīr
Because hadd is inapplicable, the perpetrator is given a discretionary punishment (ta‘zīr) determined by the judge.
Ta'zir can vary based on the judge's discretion and the circumstances.
4
Note the disposal of the animal
There is a tradition that the animal should be slaughtered and burned to prevent gossip, but the text emphasizes that this is not obligatory (wājib).
The author uses the phrase 'wa laysa bi wājib' to indicate the non-obligatory nature.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi‘i) consider bestiality punishable by hadd like zina. The Hanafi school holds the opposite due to the lack of analogy.
This illustrates the importance of usul al-fiqh in deriving rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وببهيمة) أي لا يحد بوطء بهيمة لأنه ليس في معنى الزنا في كونه جناية وفي وجود الداعي لأن الطبع السليم ينفر عنه والحامل عليه نهاية السفه أو فرط الشبق ولهذا لا يجب ستره إلا أنه يعزر لما بينا والذي يروي أنها تذبح البهيمة وتحرق لقطع التحدث به وليس بواجب. قالوا : إن كانت الدابة مما لا يؤكل لحمها تذبح وتحرق لما ذكرنا، وإن كانت مما تؤكل تذبح وتؤكل عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : تحرق هذه أيضاً. هذا إن كانت البهيمة للفاعل، فإن كانت لغيره ففي الخانية كان لصاحبها أن يدفعها إليه بالقيمة، وفي التبيين يطالب صاحبها أن يدفعها إليه بالقيمة ثم تذبح هكذا ذكروا ولا يعرف ذلك إلا سماعاً فيحمل عليه ا هـ . والظاهر أنه لا يجبر على دفعها .
His saying: (and with an animal) i.e., no hadd is imposed for intercourse with an animal, because it is not analogous to zina in being an offense and in the existence of motive, since sound nature shrinks from it, and what prompts it is extreme foolishness or excessive lust; therefore it is not obligatory to conceal it, but he is disciplined (ta'zir) as we have mentioned. And it is narrated that the animal is slaughtered and burned to cut off talk about it, and that is not obligatory. They said: If the animal is of a type whose meat is not eaten, it is slaughtered and burned as we mentioned; and if it is of a type whose meat is eaten, it is slaughtered and eaten according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: This one (the edible one) is also burned. This if the animal belongs to the perpetrator. If it belongs to someone else, then in al-Khaniyyah: the owner can hand it over to him for its price. In al-Tabyin: the owner is requested to hand it over for its price, then it is slaughtered. This is what they mentioned, and it is not known except by hearing (samā‘an), so it is understood accordingly. End. And the apparent view is that he is not compelled to hand it over.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the ruling on bestiality (intercourse with an animal). The author states that no fixed hadd punishment is imposed because the act is not analogous to zina in terms of being a crime in the sense of violating lineage, and the natural disposition (tab‘ salīm) recoils from it. The only motive is extreme foolishness or excessive lust. Therefore, the perpetrator is only subject to discretionary punishment (ta'zir). Additionally, there is a tradition that the animal involved should be slaughtered and burned to prevent people from talking about the incident, but this is not obligatory. There is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the disposal of the animal if its meat is permissible to eat: Abu Hanifa says it can be slaughtered and eaten, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it must also be burned. If the animal belongs to someone else, the owner may demand its price from the perpetrator, but the text doubts the authenticity of this and states that the owner cannot be forced to hand over the animal.

Key Terms
البهيمة (al-bahīmah): Any non-human animal.
التعزير (al-ta‘zīr): Discretionary punishment less than hadd, determined by the judge.
الذبح والحرق (al-dhabḥ wa al-ḥarq): Slaughtering and burning the animal involved in bestiality to prevent infamy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the punishment for bestiality?
A) Hadd (fixed punishment) like zina
B) Ta'zir (discretionary punishment)
C) Execution
D) No punishment at all

Key Takeaway
Bestiality is haram but does not incur hadd; the perpetrator receives ta'zir, and the animal is dealt with per the preferred Hanafi view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Disposal of the Animal After Bestiality
Intra-Hanafi dispute on animal disposal after bestiality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) on the disposal of the animal after bestiality and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa says edible animal may be eaten after slaughter; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require it to be burned.

This passage continues with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding what to do with the animal involved in bestiality. The main disagreement is whether an animal whose meat is halal can be eaten after slaughter or must be burned. Additionally, the passage discusses the situation when the animal belongs to someone else and whether the owner can demand its price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue is the disposal of the animal after bestiality, specifically when the animal's meat is halal to eat.
All agree that if the meat is haram, it is slaughtered and burned.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that if the animal is edible (halal), it is slaughtered and may be eaten.
The text says: 'وإن كانت مما تؤكل تذبح وتؤكل عند أبي حنيفة'.
3
State the position of the two companions (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that even if the animal is edible, it must be burned after slaughter.
The text says: 'وقالا : تحرق هذه أيضاً'.
4
Analyze the basis of the difference
The difference appears to stem from whether the disposal (slaughter and burn) is a mandatory punishment or merely a recommended measure to stop gossip. Abu Hanifa sees it as not obligatory, hence the animal can be eaten; the companions view it as a necessary measure to prevent the act from being trivialized.
The earlier part stated the disposal is 'not obligatory' (wa laysa bi wājib), supporting Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Examine the ruling when the animal belongs to another
If the animal is not the perpetrator's, the owner may demand its price. The text notes uncertainty about this rule, with the apparent view that the owner cannot be forced to hand it over.
The author doubts the reliability of this transmission (samā‘an) and suggests the owner is not compelled.

The Ruling of Hadd in Dar al-Harb and Dar al-Bughah
Ruling that zina committed in enemy or rebel territory does not incur hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that hadd is not applied for zina in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Bughah, identify the hadith proof, and explain the exception for the army camp.

Core Idea
Hadd punishment requires Islamic authority; where authority is absent, the punishment is not applied.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning the application of hadd (fixed corporal punishment) for zina. The key principle is that the hadd penalty is contingent on the presence of Islamic legal authority (wilayat al-imam). When the act occurs in territories where such authority is lacking, the punishment is not imposed, even if the perpetrator later returns to Muslim lands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text states clearly that hadd is not obligatory for zina committed in Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) or Dar al-Bughah (rebel territory). This is a definitive ruling in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the primary proof
The proof cited is the hadith: 'لا تقام الحدود في دار الحرب' (Hudud are not established in the abode of war). This is a direct command from the Prophet (peace be upon him).
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear according to the Hanafis.
3
Understand the teleological reasoning
The rationale provided is that the purpose of hadd is deterrence (zajr), and without the Imam's authority, deterrence cannot be achieved. The obligation becomes futile.
This reasoning aligns with the principle of maqasid al-shari'ah.
4
Analyze the exception for the army camp
If zina occurs within the Muslim army camp while stationed in Dar al-Harb, the commanding officer (with authority similar to the caliph or amir of the city) can establish the hadd because his authority is present within the camp.
This exception applies only during battle days before conquest, and only if the zina occurs inside the camp, not outside.
5
Distinguish the scope and exceptions
Note that the ruling applies even after leaving those territories; the obligation never arose. Also, amirs of small detachments do not have authority to establish hadd unless explicitly delegated.
This distinction prevents confusion about the limits of military authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبزنا في دار حرب أو بغي) أي لا يجب الحد بالزنا في دار الحرب أو في دار البغي لقوله عليه السلام لا تقام الحدود في دار الحرب» ولأن المقصود هو الانزجاز وولاية الإمام منقطعة فيهما فيعرى الوجوب عن الفائدة أطلقه فأفاد أنه لا يقام بعد الخروج أيضاً لأنها لم تنعقد موجبة فلا تنقلب موجبة. قيد بدار الحرب والبغي لأن من زنى في محل نزول العسكر فإن له ولاية الإقامة بنفسه كالخليفة وأمير مصره أن يقيم الحد عليه لأنه تحت يده من بخلاف أمير العسكر والسرية لأنه لم يفوض إليهما الإقامة ويستثنى من كلام المصنف ما لو زنى في العسكر والعسكر في دار الحرب في أيام المحاربة قبل الفتح له أن يقيمه للولاية حينئذ بخلاف ما إذا زنى واحد منهم خارج العسكر فإنه لا يقيم الحد عليه
His saying: (and with zina in Dar al-Harb or Bughy) i.e., hadd is not obligatory for zina in the abode of war or in the abode of rebellion, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Hudud are not established in the abode of war.' And because the objective is deterrence, and the authority of the Imam is severed in both, so obligation becomes devoid of benefit. He (the author) made it absolute, indicating that it is not established even after exiting, because it did not become obligatory, so it does not become obligatory later. He restricted it to Dar al-Harb and Bughy, because one who commits zina in a place where the army is stationed has the authority to establish it himself, like the caliph or the amir of his city, to establish the hadd on him because he is under his hand. Unlike the amir of the army and the detachment, because the authority to establish hadd was not delegated to them. An exception from the author's words is if he committed zina in the army while the army is in Dar al-Harb during battle days before conquest, it is permissible for him to establish it due to the authority then, unlike if one of them committed zina outside the army, then he does not establish the hadd on him.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that no hadd punishment is imposed for zina committed in Dar al-Harb (the abode of war, i.e., non-Muslim territory) or Dar al-Bughy (rebel territory). The reason is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Hudud are not established in the abode of war.' The purpose of hadd is deterrence, and the Imam's authority is absent in those lands, so the obligation would be futile. This applies even after leaving those lands, because the obligation never arose. However, if the zina occurs within the Muslim army camp (when the army is stationed in Dar al-Harb), the commanding officer (like the caliph or amir) can establish the hadd because his authority is present within the camp. But the amir of the army or a small detachment does not have that authority unless explicitly delegated. There is an exception: if the zina occurs within the army during battle days before conquest, the hadd may be established due to the effective authority at that time. If the zina occurs outside the army camp (e.g., in the enemy territory), the hadd is not established.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dār al-Ḥarb): Territory not under Muslim rule, with which Muslims are in a state of war.
دار البغي (Dār al-Bughy): Territory controlled by rebels (bughāh) who have an alternative leader and do not submit to the legitimate Imam.
ولاية الإمام (Wilāyat al-Imām): The authority of the Imam (leader) to implement Islamic legal punishments.
الانزجار (al-Inzijār): Deterrence; the purpose of hadd is to deter others from committing the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is hadd not applicable for zina committed in Dar al-Harb?
A) Because the act is not considered zina there
B) Because the authority of the Imam is lacking and the deterrent purpose is lost
C) Because the person might have been coerced
D) Because the person is not a Muslim

Key Takeaway
The hadd punishment for zina is not applicable in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Bughah due to the lack of Imam's authority, but an exception applies for the army camp.

Zina of Musta'min with Dhimmiyya: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Hadd
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for musta'min's zina with dhimmī

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on hadd for a musta'min who commits zina with a dhimmī woman, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A musta'min is not flogged for zina because it is a right of Allah, not a civil claim.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a subtle intra-madhhab dispute: does a musta'min (a non-Muslim with temporary safe passage) incur the hadd punishment for zina if he commits it with a dhimmī woman? The disagreement turns on the nature of the musta'min's commitment to Islamic law and the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of individuals. By tracing the arguments, you will understand how Hanafi jurists used underlying principles to derive nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main issue
What is the legal question being debated? (Hadd for musta'min on zina with dhimmī)
Focus on the musta'min's liability.
2
State the positions of the three Hanafi imams
Who says hadd is not imposed? Who says it is? Note any distinctions between the musta'min and the dhimmī woman.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no hadd on musta'min. Abu Yusuf later: yes hadd. For the woman: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say she is flogged; Muhammad says no.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Why did he equate musta'min to dhimmī? What evidence does he give?
He says musta'min 'undertook our laws' in transactions, and is subject to qadhf and qisas, so why not zina hadd?
4
Analyze the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
What distinguishes the musta'min from the dhimmī? Why does zina hadd not apply?
They argue: musta'min entered for temporary need, he is not a resident; hadd of zina is haqq Allah, not a civil right; he only undertakes what serves his objective (justice in civil matters).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
What difference does this opinion make in practice? Which position is mufta bihi?
Mufta bihi: no hadd on musta'min. The woman is generally flogged (except Muhammad's view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبزنا بذمية في حقه) أي لا يجب الحد بزنا رجل حربي مستأمن بذمية في حق الحربي المستأمن عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف آخراً يحد لأن المستأمن التزم أحكامنا مدة مقامه في دارنا في المعاملات كما أن الذمي التزمها مدة عمره ولهذا يحد حد القذف ويقتل قصاصاً بخلاف حد الشرب لأنه يعتقد إباحته ولهما أنه ما دخل للقرار بل لحاجته كالتجارة ونحوها فلم يصر من أهل دارنا ولهذا يمكن من الرجوع إلى دار الحرب، ولا يقتل المسلم ولا الذمي به فإنما يلتزم من الحكم ما يرجع إلى تحصيل مقصوده وهو حقوق العباد لأنه لما طمع في الإنصاف يلتزم الانتصاف والقصاص وحد القذف من حقوقهم، أما حد الزنا فمحض حق الشرع. قيد بقوله في حقه لأن الذمية تحد عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: لا تحد أيضاً لأن المرأة تابعة فامتناع الحد في حق الأصل يوجب امتناعه في حق التبع كالبالغة إذا مكنت الصبي والمجنون قلنا إن فعل المستأمن زنا لأنه مخاطب بالحرمات على ما هو الصحيح، وإن لم يكن مخاطباً بالشرائع على أصلنا والتمكين من فعل هو زنا موجب للحد عليها. وقيد بالحربي لأن الذمي إذا زنى بحربية فإنه يحد عندهما خلافاً لمحمد، والأصل لأبي يوسف أن الحدود كلها نقام على المستأمن والمستأمنة إلا حد الشرب كما تقام على الذمي والذمية فسوى بين الذمي والحربي والمستأمن. والأصل عند الإمام الأعظم أنه لا يقام على المستأمن والمستأمنة شيء من الحدود إلا حد القذف بخلاف الذمي، ومحمد يقول كذلك في جميع ما ذكرنا إلا أنه
His saying: (and with zina of a dhimmī woman in his right) i.e., hadd is not obligatory for the zina of a ḥarbī musta'min with a dhimmī woman in respect of the ḥarbī musta'min according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf later said: he is flogged (hadd), because the musta'min undertook our laws during his stay in our abode in transactions, just as the dhimmī undertook them for his lifetime. For this reason he is flogged for qadhf (slander) and is killed for retribution, unlike the hadd of drinking because he believes it permissible. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): he did not enter for settlement but for his need, like trade and the like, so he did not become from the people of our abode; therefore he is able to return to the abode of war, and neither the Muslim nor the dhimmī is killed by him. He only undertakes from the rulings what pertains to fulfilling his objective, which is the rights of people, because when he hopes for justice, he undertakes justice and retaliation, and the hadd of qadhf is among their rights. As for the hadd of zina, it is purely the right of Allah. He restricted it with 'fi ḥaqqihi' because the dhimmī woman is flogged according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: she is also not flogged, because the woman is a follower, and the prohibition of hadd regarding the original necessitates its prohibition regarding the follower, like a mature woman if she enables a boy or an insane person. We said: The act of the musta'min is zina because he is addressed concerning prohibitions according to what is correct, even if he is not addressed concerning the laws according to our principle; and the enabling of an act that is zina necessitates the hadd upon her. And he restricted it with 'al-ḥarbī' because if a dhimmī commits zina with a ḥarbiyya, he is flogged according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) contrary to Muhammad. The principle of Abu Yusuf is that all hudud are established on the musta'min and the musta'mina except the hadd of drinking, just as they are established on the dhimmī and the dhimmīyya, so he equated between the dhimmī and the ḥarbī and the musta'min. The principle of the Greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) is that no hadd is established on the musta'min and the musta'mina except the hadd of qadhf, unlike the dhimmī. And Muhammad says likewise in all that we mentioned except that...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a musta'min (a non-Muslim from enemy territory who enters Muslim lands with safe passage) is subject to the hadd punishment for zina if he commits it with a dhimmī woman (a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). The issue is: does the musta'min's temporary commitment to Islamic laws during his stay include the hadd for zina? According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the musta'min is not subject to hadd for zina because he only entered for a temporary need (e.g., trade), not for permanent residence. He is subject only to laws that serve his purpose of seeking justice in civil matters (like retaliation and qadhf hadd, which are rights of individuals). The hadd for zina is a pure right of Allah (ḥaqq Allāh) and thus not imposed on him. Abu Yusuf initially held the same view but later changed his position, equating the musta'min with the dhimmī in most hudud except drinking (since the musta'min may consider it permissible). Therefore, he would impose hadd for zina on the musta'min. There is also a sub-dispute regarding the dhimmī woman: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, she is subject to hadd because she enabled an act of zina; but Muhammad argues she is not, because the hadd is not on the man (the musta'min), and she follows him. Furthermore, if the roles are reversed (dhimmī man with a ḥarbiyya woman), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agree that the dhimmī man is subject to hadd, while Muhammad disagrees. The passage concludes by stating the two principles: Abu Yusuf's principle equates musta'min with dhimmī in most hudud; Abu Hanifa's principle exempts the musta'min from all hudud except qadhf.

Key Terms
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A non-Muslim from enemy territory who enters Muslim lands with a safe passage (amān) for a temporary purpose like trade.
الذمية (al-dhimmīyah): A non-Muslim woman living permanently in the Islamic state under a pact (dhimmah).
حق الشرع (ḥaqq al-shar‘): A right of Allah (ḥaqq Allāh), as opposed to rights of individuals (ḥuqūq al-‘ibād).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the mufta bihi Hanafi position on a musta'min who commits zina with a dhimmī woman?
A) Both are flogged.
B) The musta'min is flogged, but the woman is not.
C) The musta'min is not flogged, but the woman is flogged (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
D) Neither is flogged.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min's temporary status exempts him from hudud that are pure rights of Allah, while civil penalties apply.

Bestiality and the Disposal of the Animal: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on disposal of bestiality animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the disposal of an animal used in bestiality and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An animal used in bestiality must be killed, but Abu Hanifa permits eating it if edible, while the two companions require burning it entirely.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the legal consequence of bestiality, focusing on the animal involved. The text first notes that bestiality, like other forms of zinā with shubha, does not incur the ḥadd penalty but rather taʿzīr. It then presents a key intra-Ḥanafī dispute: how to dispose of the animal, with implications for property rights and benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal treatment of bestiality
Understand that bestiality is a sin but does not invoke the ḥadd penalty; instead, it requires taʿzīr (discretionary punishment).
This is because a shubha (semblance of legality) prevents the ḥadd.
2
Recognize the default ruling for the animal
The matn states the animal is burned. This is the starting point for analysis.
3
Differentiate the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that if the animal is edible, it should be slaughtered and eaten. The two companions insist on burning even if edible.
The key distinction is preservation of benefit vs. complete destruction.
4
Consider ownership scenarios
If the animal belongs to the perpetrator, the above ruling applies. If it belongs to another, the owner may transfer it for value (according to al-Khaniyah) or must transfer it (according to al-Tabyin), but the owner is not forced to do so.
This protects property rights.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: followers of Abu Hanifa may consume the meat; followers of the Sahibayn must destroy it completely.
The mufta bihi position for edible animals is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولواطة وببهيمة وبزنا في دار حرب أو بغى وبزنا حربي بذمية في حقه وبزنا صبي وتحرق لما ذكرنا، وإن كانت مما تؤكل تذبح وتؤكل عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : تحرق هذه أيضاً. هذا إن كانت البهيمة للفاعل، فإن كانت لغيره ففي الخانية كان لصاحبها أن يدفعها إليه بالقيمة، وفي التبيين يطالب صاحبها أن يدفعها إليه بالقيمة ثم تذبح هكذا ذكروا ولا يعرف ذلك إلا سماعاً فيحمل عليه ا هـ . والظاهر أنه لا يجبر على دفعها .
And [cases include] liwat, bestiality, zina in dar al-harb or baghy, zina of a harbi with a dhimmi in his right, and zina of a minor; and [the animal] is burned for what we have mentioned. But if it is of the type that is eaten, it is slaughtered and eaten according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions said: Burn this one as well. This is if the animal belongs to the doer. If it belongs to another, then according to al-Khaniyah, its owner may give it to him for its value, and in al-Tabyin, its owner is demanded to give it to him for its value then it is slaughtered. Thus they mentioned, and this is not known except by hearing, so it is applied accordingly. End. And evidently, he is not forced to hand it over.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal consequences of bestiality (al-wat' bi al-bahima), a form of zinā that does not incur the ḥadd penalty but requires disciplinary punishment (taʿzīr). The text first lists several cases where ḥadd is not applied, including liwat (sodomy), bestiality, zinā in Dār al-Ḥarb or Dār al-Baghy, and zinā by a minor. The focus then shifts to the animal involved. The matn states that the animal is burned ('tuḥraq). The commentator explains that according to Imam Abū Ḥanīfa, if the animal is edible (e.g., sheep, cow), it should be slaughtered and then eaten, not burned. However, the two companions (Imam Abū Yūsuf and Imam Muḥammad) hold that it must be burned even if edible. This is an intra-Ḥanafī dispute. The author then notes that this ruling applies only if the animal belongs to the person who committed the act. If it belongs to someone else, there is a difference: according to al-Khāniyah, the owner can hand it over to the offender in exchange for its value, while in al-Tabyīn, the owner is obligated to hand it over for value, after which it is slaughtered. The author comments that this opinion is based on oral transmission (samāʿ) and is therefore accepted. Finally, he states that the owner of the animal is not forced to hand it over.

Key Terms
بهيمة (bahīma): A quadruped animal, specifically the one used in bestiality.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance of legality that prevents the ḥadd penalty.
تذبح (tudhbaḥ): To slaughter ritually, making the meat halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default ruling for the animal unless it is edible and the perpetrator follows Abu Hanifa?
A) Slaughter and eat
B) Burn
C) Set free
D) Bury alive

Key Takeaway
The animal used in bestiality must be killed; the intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether edible animals are slaughtered and eaten or burned.

Hadd Liability in Zina Involving a Child or Insane Person
Commentary explaining the ruling on zina involving a child or insane person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when a child or insane person commits zina with a legally responsible adult and explain the reasoning (talil) based on legal capacity.

Core Idea
Hadd is tied to legal capacity; a child or insane person is not liable, but the other party is.

The passage from Bahur al-Raiq comments on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq about zina when one party lacks legal capacity. The ruling differentiates based on who is mukallaf (legally responsible). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying hudud punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts: (a) a boy or insane man with a legally responsible woman, and (b) an adult man with a young girl or insane woman. Determine the hadd outcome in each.
Focus on who is mukallaf (sane, adult).
2
Understand the legal basis
The act of zina is only legally attributed to a mukallaf. A child or insane person is not addressed by the command to refrain, so their act is not considered zina in the legal sense. Hence no hadd on them.
Legal capacity (taklif) is the key condition for hadd liability.
3
Analyze the metaphorical naming
The woman is called a fornicator (zaniyah) metaphorically because she enables (tamkin) the act. Her liability arises from this enabling, even if the partner is not legally responsible.
The term 'zaniyah' in her case is not literal; it's based on causation.
4
Evaluate the attempted generalization
Some tried to make a rule: 'if hadd is negated from the man, it is negated from the woman.' The passage rejects this with counterexamples (coerced man with willing woman, musta'min with dhimmi/Muslim woman). Each case is judged by its own evidence.
General rules have exceptions; always return to the specific dalil.
5
Note the implication for ihsan
The woman's act with a boy or insane man does not nullify her ihsan (status as muhsan). Similarly, slandering a child or insane person after puberty/recovery incurs hadd for qadhf, because their ihsan was not removed.
Ihsan is only affected by zina with a legally responsible partner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبزنا صبي أو مجنون بمكلفة بخلاف عكسه أي لا يجب الحد إذا زنى صبي أو مجنون بمكلفة ويجب الحد إذا زنى بالغ بصبية أو مجنونة لأن فعل الزنا يتحقق منه وهي الفعل ولهذا يسمى هو واطئاً وزانياً والمرأة موطوءة ومزنيّاً بها إلا أنها سميت زانية مجازاً تسمية محل للفعل باسم الفاعل كالراضية بمعنى المرضية أو لكونها مسببة بالتمكين فتعلق الحد في حقها بالتمكين من قبيح الزنا وهو فعل من هو مخاطب بالكف عنه مؤثم على مباشرته، وفعل الصبي ليس بهذه الصفة فلا يناط به الحد، وقد ذكر بعضهم أن كلما انتفى الحد عن الرجل انتفى عن المرأة وهو منقوض بزنا المكره بالمطاوعة والمستأمن بالذمية والمسلمة فالأولى أن لا تجعل قاعدة لأن الحكم في كل موضع بمقتضى الدليل . قال في التبيين : وعبارات أصحابنا أن فعلها مع الصبي والمجنون ليس بزنا يشير إلى أن إحصانها لا يسقط بذلك كما لا يسقط إحصان الصبي والمجنون حتى يجب الحد على قاذفهما بعد البلوغ والإفاقة وقد قدمنا حكم المهر
His saying: (And by zina of a boy or an insane person with a legally responsible woman, contrary to the opposite) meaning the hadd is not obligatory if a boy or an insane person commits zina with a legally responsible woman, and the hadd is obligatory if an adult commits zina with a young girl or an insane woman because the act of zina actually occurs from him and she is the object of the act, and for this reason he is called an intercourse-performer and a fornicator, while the woman is called the object of intercourse and the one with whom zina is committed, except that she is called a fornicator metaphorically, naming the place of the act by the name of the doer, like al-radiyah meaning 'the one who is satisfied,' or because she is a cause by enabling, so the hadd is attached in her case due to enabling of the ugly act of zina, which is an act of one who is addressed to refrain from it and is sinful for committing it, and the act of the boy does not have this description, so the hadd is not attached to it. Some have mentioned that whenever the hadd is negated from the man, it is negated from the woman, but this is refuted by the case of zina of a coerced person with a willing woman, and of a musta'min with a dhimmi or Muslim woman, so it is better not to make it a rule because the ruling in every place is based on the evidence. He said in al-Tabyin: The statements of our companions are that her act with the boy and the insane person is not zina, indicating that her ihsan is not nullified by that, just as the ihsan of the boy and the insane person is not nullified, so that the hadd is obligatory on the one who slanders them after puberty and recovery. We have already mentioned the ruling of the bridal gift (mahr).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The issue is the hadd punishment for zina when one party is a child or insane. The ruling: if a boy (pre-pubescent) or an insane man commits zina with a sane adult woman, there is no hadd on the man, but there is hadd on the woman. Conversely, if an adult man commits zina with a young girl or insane woman, there is hadd on the man but not on the woman. The reasoning is that the act of zina is legally attributed to the one who is legally responsible (mukallaf). The boy and insane person are not addressed to refrain, so their act is not considered zina in the legal sense. The woman, however, is responsible for enabling the act (tamkin), so if she enables it with a boy, she still incurs the hadd. Some attempted to make a general rule that if the man has no hadd, the woman also has none, but this is rejected with counterexamples like a coerced man and a willing woman, or a musta'min with a dhimmi or Muslim woman. The correct approach is that each case is judged by its own evidence. The commentary also notes that according to the Hanafi school, the woman's act with a boy is not considered zina in a way that nullifies her ihsan (status as muhsan), nor does it affect the boy's ihsan. Thus, if someone slanders them after puberty/recovery, the hadd for qadhf is obligatory.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A boy who has not reached puberty, not legally responsible.
مجنون (majnoon): An insane person lacking legal capacity.
مكلفة (mukallafah): A legally responsible adult female (sane, pubescent).
تمكين (tamkin): Enabling, the woman's act of facilitating zina, which is the basis for her hadd liability.
إحصان (ihsan): The status of being muhsan (having been married and having consummated marriage), which affects certain hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an adult man commits zina with a young girl who has not reached puberty, what is the ruling on the hadd punishment?
A) Both receive hadd.
B) Only the man receives hadd.
C) Only the girl receives hadd.
D) Neither receives hadd.

Key Takeaway
Hadd for zina is based on individual legal capacity (taklif); a child or insane person is never liable, but the other party may be, and the woman's liability arises from enabling (tamkin), not from the man's liability.

Zina with a Hired Woman: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for zina with a hired woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on hadd for zina with a hired woman and explain the role of shubha in the Hanafi hudud framework.

Core Idea
A mere resemblance to a valid contract (shubha) can avert hadd, even if the act is clearly zina.

When a man hires a woman specifically to commit zina, is the hadd punishment still mandatory? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the hadd is averted due to a shubha (suspicion) arising from the Qur'anic use of 'ujr (wage) for mahr. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) insist that the hadd is obligatory because no real ownership exists. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school uses shubha to avoid punishments in hudud cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the disputed condition
The passage rules on zina where a man hires a woman specifically for that purpose. The dispute hinges on whether the hire contract creates a shubha that averts the hadd.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: no hadd because of shubha. Sahibayn: hadd is obligatory because there is no valid ownership.
The Sahibayn also note that lineage and 'idda are not established, confirming no shubha.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's dalil
He cites Qur'an 4:24 where Allah calls the bridal gift 'ajr (wage). This resemblance between mahr and hire creates a shubha that the contract is like a marriage, even though it is not valid.
Shubha is defined as what resembles reality, not reality itself.
4
Understand the qualification (taqyid)
The commentary restricts this to hiring specifically for zina. If she was hired for service and then zina occurs, hadd applies by agreement because the contract is not connected to the act.
This shows that the shubha only arises when the contract directly applies to the sexual act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالزنا بمستأجرة أي لا يجب الحد بوطء من استأجرها ليزني بها عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : يجب الحد لعدم شبهة الملك ولهذا لا يثبت النسب ولا تجب العدة. وله أن الله تعالى سمى المهر أجرة بقوله تعالى {فما استمتعتم به منهن فآتوهن أجورهن} [النساء: 24] فصار شبهة لأن الشبهة ما يشبه الحقيقة لا الحقيقة فصار كما لو قال أمهرتك كذا لأزني بك . قيدنا بأن يكون استأجرها ليزني بها لأنه لو استأجرها للخدمة فزنى بها يجب الحد اتفاقاً لأن العقد لم يضف إلى المستوفي بالوطء والعقد المضاف إلى محل يورث الشبهة في ذلك المحل لا في محل آخر
His saying: (And by zina with a hired woman) i.e., the hadd is not obligatory for intercourse with a woman he hired to commit zina with her according to Abu Hanifa. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the hadd is obligatory due to the absence of any suspicion (shubha) of ownership, and for this reason lineage is not established and 'idda is not obligatory. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that Allah the Exalted named the bridal gift (mahr) as 'ujr in His saying: {So what you have enjoyed from them, give them their wages} [4:24], so this creates a shubha because shubha is what resembles reality, not reality itself, so it becomes as if he said: I give you mahr of such-and-such for zina with you. We qualify it by stating that he hired her to commit zina with her, because if he hired her for service and then committed zina with her, the hadd is obligatory by agreement, since the contract was not connected to the benefit of intercourse, and a contract connected to a place gives shubha in that place, not in another place.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the hadd punishment when a man hires a woman specifically to commit zina with her. According to Abu Hanifa, no hadd is imposed because there is a shubha (suspicion/likeness) of a valid contract. The basis is that Allah calls mahr 'ujr (wage) in the Qur'an (4:24), so paying money for sexual enjoyment resembles a marriage contract to some extent. Even though it is not a valid marriage, the striking similarity creates a doubt that averts the hadd punishment. The Sahibayn argue that there is no shubha of ownership because no valid marriage exists, so lineage and 'idda are not established, hence hadd applies. The commentary qualifies that this ruling applies only if the hire was specifically for zina; if he hired her for service and then committed zina, the hadd is obligatory by unanimous agreement, because the contract was not connected to the immoral act.

Key Terms
مستأجرة (musta'jarah): A woman hired for sexual intercourse (specifically for zina).
شبهة (shubha): A suspicion or resemblance that raises doubt about the legal character of the act, averting the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is hadd not imposed on a man who hires a woman for zina?
A) Because the act is not zina.
B) Because the woman consented.
C) Because the mahr is called 'ujr in the Qur'an, creating a shubha.
D) Because there is a valid marriage.

Key Takeaway
A mere linguistic resemblance (shubha) can avert the hadd punishment in Hanafi law, even when the act is undoubtedly zina.

Zina under Coercion: The Hadd Dispute in the Hanafi School
Commentary on the ruling for zina under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for coerced zina and explain how the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf was resolved by later fatwa.

Core Idea
No hadd for coerced zina when coercion is overwhelming — the later fatwa follows Sahibayn without distinction.

This lesson examines a famous intra-Hanafi dispute: does a man who commits zina under coercion incur the hadd punishment? The source passage from Bahr al-Raiq reviews the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and explains how a change in socio-political circumstances led later scholars to adopt the Sahibayn's view. Understanding this case illustrates the dynamic nature of Hanafi fatwa, where changed conditions ('urf and zaman) can shift the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its scope
The passage begins with the general statement that hadd is not obligatory for zina under coercion. It covers both sultan and non-sultan coercion.
This is the starting point before the ikhtilaf.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's two views on sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa initially held that hadd applies because erection indicates willingness. Later he dropped it because the sword is overwhelming and erection can occur without intent.
This shows a shift within the same imam.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on non-sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa holds hadd applies because the victim can seek help from the sultan or community. Sahibayn argue that fear of death can come from any coercer.
The dispute is based on whether coercion from non-sultan is rare or credible.
4
Understand the historical context and tarjih
In Abu Hanifa's time, only the sultan had overwhelming power; later, many tyrants arose, making non-sultan coercion common. Hence, Sahibayn's view was adopted.
This is a clear example of fatwa changing with 'urf and zaman.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later fatwa drops hadd absolutely for any coercer, regardless of sultan or non-sultan.
This is the operative ruling today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبإكراه) أي لا يجب الحد بالزنا بإكراه أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المكره السلطان أو غيره، أما إذا كان المكره السلطان فكان أبو حنيفة أولاً يقول عليه الحد وهو قول زفر لأن الزنا من الرجل لا يتصور إلا بعد انتشار الآلة وهذا آية الطوع، ووجه قوله الآخر أن السبب الملجئ قائم ظاهراً وهو قيام السيف على رأسه والانتشار دليل محتمل لأنه قد يكون من غير قصد كما في النائم فلا يزول اليقين بالمحتمل، وأما إذا أكرهه غير السلطان فإنه يحد عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يحد لتحقق الإكراه من غير السلطان عندهما لأن المؤثر خوف الهلاك ويتحقق من غيره، وله أنه غيره لا إلا نادراً لتمكنه من الاستغاثة بالسلطان وبجماعة المسلمين ويمكنه دفع شره بنفسه بالسلاح والنادر لا حكم له فلا يسقط الحد بخلاف السلطان لأنه لا يمكنه الاستغاثة بغيره ولا الخروج بالسلاح عليه. قالوا: هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لأنه لم يمكن في زمن أبي حنيفة لغير السلطان من القوة ما لا يمكن دفعها بالسلطان، وفي زمنهما ظهرت القوة لكل متغلب فيفتى بقولهما؛ كذا في الظهيرية فلذا أطلق في المختصر
His saying: (And by coercion) i.e., the hadd is not obligatory for zina under coercion. He made it general, which includes whether the one coercing is the sultan or other. As for if the coercer is the sultan, Abu Hanifa initially used to say the hadd is upon him, and that is the view of Zafar, because zina from a man cannot be imagined except after the organ's erection, and that is a sign of willingness. And the basis of his other view is that the compelling cause is evidently present, namely the sword over his head, and the erection is a probable indicator because it can occur without intent as in a sleeping person, so certainty is not removed by probability. As for if someone other than the sultan coerces him, according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) he is subject to hadd. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not subject to hadd because coercion is verified from other than the sultan in their view, because the effective factor is fear of destruction, and that can be verified from others. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that it is rare because he can seek help from the sultan and the Muslim congregation, and he can repel his evil with his own weapon, and the rare has no ruling, so the hadd is not waived, unlike the sultan because he cannot seek help from others nor go out bearing arms against him. They said: This is a difference of era and time, because in Abu Hanifa's time it was not possible for non-sultans to have power that could not be repelled by the sultan, whereas in their time power emerged for every tyrant, so fatwa is given according to their view. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah, that is why it was made absolute in al-Mukhtasar.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on hadd for a man who commits zina under coercion (ikrah). The general ruling is that there is no hadd for coerced zina. However, there is a detailed dispute: if the coercer is the sultan (ruler), Abu Hanifa had two views—one that hadd applies because erection indicates willingness, and another that hadd is dropped due to the overwhelming duress. The latter is preferred. If the coercer is someone other than the sultan, Abu Hanifa holds that hadd applies because the victim can seek help from the sultan or the community, so coercion is rare. The Sahibayn hold that hadd is not imposed because genuine fear of death can come from anyone. The commentary notes that this difference is historically contextual: in Abu Hanifa's time, only the sultan had overwhelming power, so non-sultan coercion was unlikely. In later times, many tyrants arose, so the Sahibayn's view is followed. Hence, the author of al-Mukhtasar stated the ruling absolutely (dropping hadd without distinction).

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will, specifically involving threat of death or grievous harm.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law for certain crimes, such as zina.
إكراه ملجئ (ikrah mulji'): Overwhelming coercion that leaves no choice, typically threat of death or loss of limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later fatwa (mufta bihi), what is the ruling on hadd for a man forced to commit zina by a non-sultan?
A) Hadd is obligatory.
B) Hadd is dropped.
C) Hadd is reduced to ta'zir.
D) It depends on whether the coerced person had an erection.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on hadd for coerced zina evolved from distinguishing sultan vs. non-sultan to a blanket exemption for all overwhelming coercion, reflecting the principle that fatwa can adapt to changing circumstances.

Hadd for Zina Dropped When One Party Denies
Commentary on zina confession and denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when one party confesses to zina and the other denies, and explain the role of shubha in averting hadd.

Core Idea
Denial by one party creates shubha that the act may be halal marriage, thus hadd is dropped for both (Abu Hanifa).

Hudud penalties are averted by doubts (shubuhat). This lesson examines a key application: when one person confesses to zina but the other denies it. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the denial creates a shubha that the act might have been a valid marriage, so the hadd is not imposed on either party. The passage also presents the view of the Sahibayn, who qualify this ruling, and extends it to special cases like a mute woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its condition
The passage begins with the general rule: no hadd when one party confesses and the other denies. Identify the condition that triggers the ruling (the denial) and the legal result (hadd dropped).
Note that the ruling applies regardless of whether the denier is the man or the woman.
2
Understand the basis: shubha and reverence for the pudendum
The underlying reason (talil) is that zina is a joint act, so denial by one side creates a possibility of marriage, which is a shubha. The hadd is dropped out of reverence for the sanctity of the private parts (al-bida'). This principle is central to Hanafi hudud jurisprudence.
The text says: 'because the claim of marriage carries the possibility of truth, and it arises from both sides, so it creates a shubha.'
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Sahibayn's qualification
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differ: they require the denier to claim a shubha (e.g., 'I married him') for hadd to be dropped. If the denier merely denies the act without such a claim, the confessor alone faces hadd. This is a key point of difference within the Hanafi school.
The text distinguishes: 'if he denies by saying 'I did not commit zina' and does not claim what drops the hadd, then hadd on the confessor.'
4
Apply the ruling to special cases
The passage extends the ruling to a mute woman (no hadd on either) and distinguishes cases where the woman is insane or a minor (hadd only on man) or absent (hadd on confessor alone). Trace the reasoning for each case based on the presence or absence of shubha.
In al-Asl, the mute woman's denial is treated like a speaking woman's claim of marriage, preserving shubha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبإقرار إن أنكره الآخر أي لا يجب الحد بإقرار أحد الزانيين إذا أنكره الآخر لأن دعوى النكاح يحتمل الصدق وهو يقوم بالطرفين فأورث شبهة، وإذا سقط تعظيماً لخطر البضع أطلقه فشمل ما إذا قال لم أطأ أصلاً أو قال تزوجت، وشمل ما إذا كان المنكر الرجل أو المرأة وهو قول الإمام. وقالا : إن ادعى المنكر منهما الشبهة بأن قال تزوجته فهو كما قال وإن أنكر بأن قال ما زنيت ولم يدع ما يسقط الحد وجب على المقر الحد دون المنكر. وحاصل دليل الإمام أن الزنا فعل مشترك بينهما قائم بهما فانتفاؤه عن أحدهما يورث شبهة في الآخر، وإذا سقط الحد وجب المهر تعظيماً لأمر البضع، وإن كانت هي من ضرورة سقوط الحد. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو زنى بامرأة خرساء منكرة لأمر النكاح لا حد على واحد منهما . قال في الأصل وجعل الجواب في الخرساء كالجواب فيما إذا كانت المرأة ناطقة وادعت المرأة النكاح بخلاف ما إذا كانت المرأة مجنونة أو صبية يجامع مثلها كان على الرجل الحمد، وبخلاف ما إذا كانت المرأة غائبة وأقر الرجل أنه زنى بها أو شهد عليه الشهود فإنه يقام الحد على الرجل ؛ كذا في الظهيرية
His saying: (And by confession if the other denies) i.e., the hadd is not obligatory by the confession of one of the two fornicators when the other denies it, because the claim of marriage carries the possibility of truth, and it arises from both sides, so it creates a shubha. And since it is dropped out of reverence for the importance of the pudendum, it is general, including when he says 'I did not have intercourse at all' or 'I married,' and it includes whether the denier is the man or the woman. This is the view of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If the denier from among them claims a shubha by saying 'I married him,' then it is as he said, but if he denies by saying 'I did not commit zina' and does not claim that which drops the hadd, then the hadd becomes obligatory on the confessor but not on the denier. The summary of the Imam's proof is that zina is a joint act occurring from both, so its negation from one creates a shubha in the other. And when the hadd is dropped, the mahr becomes obligatory out of reverence for the matter of the pudendum, even if it is a consequence of the dropping of the hadd. The author indicated that if he committed zina with a mute woman who denies the matter of marriage, there is no hadd on either of them. He said in al-Asl: He made the answer in the case of the mute woman like the answer when the woman is speaking and claims marriage, unlike if the woman is insane or a young girl who is fit for intercourse, then the hadd is due on the man. And unlike if the woman is absent and the man confessed to zina with her or witnesses testified, then the hadd is established on the man. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where one party confesses to zina but the other denies it. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the hadd is not imposed on either party because the denial creates a shubha (doubt) that the act might have been a valid marriage. The hadd is averted out of reverence for the sanctity of the pudendum (bida'). The ruling applies whether the denier is the man or the woman, and whether the confessor's claim is 'I did not have intercourse' or 'I married.' The Sahibayn differentiate: if the denier claims a shubha (e.g., marriage), the hadd is dropped; but if the denier simply denies the act without any alternative claim, then the confessor alone faces hadd. The author (of the matn) also mentions the case of a mute woman: if she denies marriage, no hadd on either. In al-Asl, the ruling for a mute woman is like that of a speaking woman who claims marriage. However, if the woman is insane or a minor, the man alone is liable for hadd. Similarly, if the man confesses but the woman is absent, or if witnesses testify against the man, the hadd is imposed on the man alone. The overall principle is that doubt in the form of denial or possibility of marriage averted the hadd.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession of committing zina.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt that prevents the application of hadd; here the possibility of marriage.
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed penalty for zina (stoning or lashing).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a mute woman who denies zina?
A) Her denial is ignored, hadd is imposed on the man.
B) Her denial is treated like a speaking woman's claim of marriage, so no hadd on either.
C) She is considered incapable of giving consent, so hadd on both.
D) Only the woman receives hadd.

Key Takeaway
When one party denies zina, the resulting shubha averts hadd for both, though the Sahibayn require a specific claim of shubha for this protection.

Zina Under Coercion: Authority of Imam vs. Sahibayn
Explanation of when coercion negates hadd, with internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on hadd under coercion by a non-sultan and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A man coerced by a non-sultan may avoid hadd if coercion is genuine, yet the later fatwa follows Sahibayn (no hadd) due to changing social conditions.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of coercion (ikrah) on the hadd penalty for zina. The central question is whether the authority of the coercer matters. Abu Hanifa initially held that coercion by non-sultan is too rare to waive hadd, while the Sahibayn argued that genuine fear of death can come from anyone. Later Hanafi scholars reconciled these views by recognising that social conditions had changed, making non-sultan coercion more common, and thus the operative fatwa now follows the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the ruling of the matn
The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that hadd is not imposed under coercion. The commentator broadens it to include any coercer. Note that the initial ruling is absolute.
The matn does not distinguish between sultan and non-sultan.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa’s initial and later positions on sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa first said hadd is imposed even under sultan coercion (following Zufar), based on arousal indicating willingness. His later view waives hadd because the threat is manifest and arousal may be involuntary (like sleep).
The later view is the one relied upon for sultan coercion.
3
State the dispute for non-sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa: hadd is imposed because the victim can seek help or defend himself, making genuine coercion rare. Sahibayn: hadd is not imposed because fear of death can be caused by anyone and help may be unavailable.
This is the core ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the historical reconciliation
Later scholars (e.g., authors of al-Hidayah, al-Zahiriyya) noted that in Abu Hanifa’s time, non-sultan tyrants lacked power, but in the Sahibayn’s time, tyrants arose, making coercion common. Thus, fatwa shifted to the Sahibayn view.
This is a difference of era (ikhtilaf al-'asr wa al-zaman).
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
Today, for any coercion (sultan or non-sultan), hadd is not imposed. This is the operative position because it follows the Sahibayn and reflects ease (takhfif).
The matn of al-Mukhtasar (al-Quduri) made the ruling absolute based on this later view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبإكراه) أي لا يجب الحد بالزنا بإكراه أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المكره السلطان أو غيره، أما إذا كان المكره السلطان فكان أبو حنيفة أولاً يقول عليه الحد وهو قول زفر لأن الزنا من الرجل لا يتصور إلا بعد انتشار الآلة وهذا آية الطوع، ووجه قوله الآخر أن السبب الملجئ قائم ظاهراً وهو قيام السيف على رأسه والانتشار دليل محتمل لأنه قد يكون من غير قصد كما في النائم فلا يزول اليقين بالمحتمل، وأما إذا أكرهه غير السلطان فإنه يحد عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يحد لتحقق الإكراه من غير السلطان عندهما لأن المؤثر خوف الهلاك ويتحقق من غيره، وله أنه غيره لا إلا نادراً لتمكنه يدوم . الاستغاثة بالسلطان من وبجماعة المسلمين ويمكنه دفع شره بنفسه بالسلاح والنادر لا حكم له فلا يسقط الحد بخلاف السلطان لأنه لا يمكنه الاستغاثة بغيره ولا الخروج بالسلاح عليه. قالوا: هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لأنه لم يمكن في زمن أبي حنيفة لغير السلطان من القوة ما لا يمكن دفعها بالسلطان، وفي زمنهما ظهرت القوة لكل متغلب فيفتى بقولهما؛ كذا في الظهيرية فلذا أطلق في المختصر .
His statement: (And by coercion) i.e., hadd is not obligatory for zina under coercion. He made it general, so it includes whether the coercer is the sultan or someone else. As for when the coercer is the sultan, Abu Hanifa initially said hadd is upon him – and this is Zufar's view – because zina from a man is not conceivable except after the arousal of the organ, and this is a sign of willingness. His other view is that the compelling cause is manifestly present, i.e., the sword over his head, and arousal is a probable evidence because it may be without intention, as in the sleeping person, so certainty is not removed by probability. As for when someone other than the sultan coerces him, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) he is penalized. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not penalized because coercion from other than the sultan is established in their view, since the effective factor is fear of death, and that can be realized from anyone other than him. His (Imam's) argument is that other than the sultan is not (effective) except rarely because he can continuously seek help from the sultan and the congregation of Muslims, and he can repel his evil by himself with weapons. The rare has no ruling, so hadd is not waived, contrary to the sultan, because he cannot seek help from others nor fight against him with weapons. They said: This is a difference of era and time, because in Abu Hanifa's time it was not possible for other than the sultan to have power that cannot be repelled by the sultan, while in their time power appeared for every tyrant, so fatwa is given according to their view; thus in al-Zahiriyya, and therefore he (the author of al-Mukhtasar) made it absolute.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of coercion (ikrah) on the hadd penalty for zina. The text from 'al-Bahr al-Raiq' comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which states that hadd is not required when there is coercion. The commentator explains that this ruling applies whether the coercer is the sultan or someone else. Initially, Abu Hanifa held that if the sultan coerces, the hadd is still required, a view also held by Zufar, because the man's arousal is a sign of willingness. However, his later view (and the relied-upon one) is that the hadd is waived because the threat of death (sword over head) is a manifest compelling cause, and arousal could occur without intent (like in sleep). For coercion by others, Abu Hanifa maintained the hadd, because the person can seek help from the sultan or defend himself, making coercion rare. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argued that coercion by others is possible and fear of death is real, so hadd should be waived. Later scholars reconciled this by noting that conditions changed: in Abu Hanifa's time, non-sultan coercers were rare due to the sultan's power, but later, tyrants emerged, so the fatwa now follows the Sahibayn view. The textbook mentions that 'al-Mukhtasar' (by al-Quduri) made the ruling absolute (i.e., that hadd is waived for any coercion) based on this later view.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion that compels a person to commit zina under threat of death or grievous harm, negating hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa, what is the ruling on a man who commits zina under coercion by a powerful bandit (non-sultan)?
A) Hadd is imposed because the man could have sought help.
B) Hadd is not imposed because coercion is genuine and fear of death is effective.
C) Hadd is imposed only if the man was aroused.
D) Hadd is not imposed only if the coercer is the sultan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school’s fatwa on zina under coercion evolved from Abu Hanifa’s distinction (sultan vs. non-sultan) to the Sahibayn’s absolute waiver, reflecting a sensitivity to changing social conditions.

Zina Confession and Denial – Shubha from the Other Party
Ruling and internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on hadd when one confesses and the other denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when one party confesses to zina and the other denies, and explain the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Denial of zina by one party creates a shubha of marriage, preventing hadd on both – but only according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Hudud are punishments for grave sins, but the Shari'ah is cautious in applying them. This lesson examines a subtle scenario: what happens when only one person confesses to zina and the other denies it? The denial may hint at a lawful marriage claim, creating doubt (shubha). Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students differ on the outcome – a classic intra-madhhab debate that highlights the school's principles of shubha and caution in hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Matn's Ruling
Read the matn phrase: 'وبإقرار إن أنكره الآخر' – the ruling is that hadd is not imposed if the other party denies. Note that the matn does not specify the gender of the denier; it applies to both.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq; the commentary expands it.
2
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa's Position and His Dalil
Abu Hanifa (the Imam) holds that the denial itself automatically creates a shubha of a valid marriage. Since zina is a joint act, the denial by one casts doubt on the whole act. Thus hadd is waived for both, and the confessor must pay the mahr (dower) to honor the woman's private part.
The Imam's reasoning is dalil aqli (rational): the shared nature of the act means negation by one affiliate negates the whole.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's Position and Their Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) distinguish: if the denier claims a shubha (e.g., says 'I married him'), then hadd falls. But if the denier simply denies the act without claiming a lawful basis, then hadd is imposed solely on the confessor; the denier is not punished because his/her word is accepted against the confessor's admission.
The Sahibayn focus on the express claim of shubha, not an implied one.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Special Cases
The practical difference (thamarah) is clear: in a case where a man confesses and a woman denies without claiming marriage, Abu Hanifa says no hadd on either and mahr is due; the Sahibayn say hadd on the man. The commentary then lists special cases: mute woman (no hadd, like an articulate claimant), insane/minor woman (hadd on man), absent woman (hadd on man if he confesses or witnesses testify). These follow the same logic: shubha exists when the woman could claim marriage; if she cannot (insanity, absence), no shubha for the man.
These exceptions show the boundary of the shubha principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبإقرار إن أنكره الآخر) أي لا يجب الحد بإقرار أحد الزانيين إذا أنكره الآخر لأن دعوى النكاح يحتمل الصدق وهو يقوم بالطرفين فأورث شبهة، وإذا سقط تعظيماً لخطر البضع أطلقه فشمل ما إذا قال لم أطأ أصلاً أو قال تزوجت، وشمل ما إذا كان المنكر الرجل أو المرأة وهو قول الإمام. وقالا : إن ادعى المنكر منهما الشبهة بأن قال تزوجته فهو كما قال وإن أنكر بأن قال ما زنيت ولم يدع ما يسقط الحد وجب على المقر الحد دون المنكر. وحاصل دليل الإمام أن الزنا فعل مشترك بينهما قائم بهما فانتفاؤه عن أحدهما يورث شبهة في الآخر، وإذا سقط الحد وجب المهر تعظيماً لأمر البضع، وإن كانت هي من ضرورة سقوط الحد. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو زنى بامرأة خرساء منكرة لأمر النكاح لا حد على واحد منهما . قال في الأصل وجعل الجواب في الخرساء كالجواب فيما إذا كانت المرأة ناطقة وادعت المرأة النكاح بخلاف ما إذا كانت المرأة مجنونة أو صبية يجامع مثلها كان على الرجل الحمد، وبخلاف ما إذا كانت المرأة غائبة وأقر الرجل أنه زنى بها أو شهد عليه الشهود فإنه يقام الحد على الرجل ؛ كذا في الظهيرية
His statement: (And by confession if the other denies) i.e., the hadd is not obligatory by the confession of one of the two adulterers if the other denies it, because the claim of marriage could be truthful and it is established by both sides, thus it creates a shubha. And when it (hadd) falls due to honoring the gravity of the pudendum, he made it general, so it includes when he said 'I did not have intercourse at all' or he said 'I married her', and includes whether the denier is the man or the woman – this is the view of the Imam. They (the two) said: If the denier among them claims a shubha by saying 'I married him', then it is as he said; but if he denies by saying 'I did not commit zina' and does not claim something that nullifies the hadd, then the hadd is obligatory upon the confessor but not on the denier. The gist of the Imam's proof is that zina is an act shared between them, carried out by both, so its negation from one of them creates a shubha in the other. And when the hadd falls, the dower (mahr) becomes obligatory to honor the affair of the pudendum, even though it (mahr) is a necessary consequence of the fall of hadd. The author indicated that if he commits zina with a mute woman who denies the matter of marriage, there is no hadd on either of them. He said in al-Asl: And he made the answer concerning the mute woman like the answer when the woman is articulate and she claims marriage, contrary to when the woman is insane or a minor who is fit for intercourse – then hadd is upon the man; and contrary to when the woman is absent and the man confesses that he committed zina with her or witnesses testify against him, then the hadd is established upon the man; as in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal effect of a confession (iqrar) to zina when the other party denies it. The matn in Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that hadd is not imposed if the other party denies. The commentator explains that this is because the denial can be interpreted as a claim of marriage, creating a shubha (doubt). This applies whether the denier is male or female according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) distinguish: if the denier claims a shubha (e.g., marriage), hadd falls; if the denier merely denies the act without claiming a valid excuse, then hadd is imposed on the confessor (but not on the denier). The Imam's reasoning is that zina is a joint act; denying it by one partner casts doubt on the whole act, so hadd is waived for both, and instead the confessor must pay the dower (mahr) to compensate for the sexual relationship. Special cases: if the woman is mute and denies marriage, there is no hadd; if she is insane or a minor, hadd is on the man; if the woman is absent and the man confesses or witnesses testify, hadd is applied to the man. These nuances are from al-Zahiriyya.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Confession by one party that zina occurred, which alone is insufficient for hadd if the other denies.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt that prevents the application of hadd, here arising from the possibility of a valid marriage claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a man confesses to zina with a woman and she denies without claiming marriage, what is the ruling?
A) Hadd is imposed on the man only.
B) Hadd is imposed on both.
C) Hadd is not imposed on either, and the man must pay the dower.
D) Hadd is not imposed only if the woman claims marriage.

Key Takeaway
Denial by one party in a bilateral act creates a shubha that, according to the majority Hanafi view, averts the hadd punishment for both parties.

Zina with Slave Woman Followed by Killing – Hadd and Value
Ruling on combined liability for hadd and value when a slave woman is killed during zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combined hadd and value when a slave woman is killed during zina, explain the qiyas of Abu Yusuf, and evaluate why the majority view does not treat the value guarantee as a shubha.

Core Idea
Two separate crimes require two separate penalties—the killing guarantee does not create a shubha for hadd.

This lesson examines a nuanced case where two crimes coincide: zina with a slave woman and her subsequent death due to the act. The central question is whether the obligation to pay her value creates a shubha (doubt) that would waive the hadd penalty. We will explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the majority, and the qiyas principles that underlie the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the general ruling
Note the scenario: a man commits zina with a slave woman and kills her. The source states that both hadd for zina and payment of the slave's value (qimah) are obligatory. Understand that two distinct crimes are involved.
The killing may be direct or as a result of the zina act.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that the hadd should be dropped because the obligation to pay the value creates a cause for ownership, similar to buying the slave after zina. Recognize that this is a qiyas on the case of theft: if a thief buys stolen property before amputation, the hadd is waived.
This is a dalil aqli reasoning based on the concept of shubha.
3
Analyze the majority's response
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond that the guarantee for killing is not a guarantee for the slave woman herself but for the loss of life. They distinguish between guarantee for a specific ayn (physical object) and guarantee for usufruct or life, which does not transfer ownership.
They use the example of destroying an eye: there the guarantee creates ownership of the blind body (ayn), hence shubha; here the usufruct is already consumed.
4
Compare with the eye destruction case
Consider the distinction: if the man destroys the slave woman's eye, he pays her value and the hadd falls because ownership of the ayn creates a shubha. In the killing case, the subject is the life, not a specific property, so no shubha.
This clarifies the precise illa (effective cause) for shubha.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The commentary indicates that the established view (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that both hadd and value are required. Abu Yusuf's view is recorded as a dissent. The mufta bihi follows the majority.
Practical consequence: the offender faces both penalties.

قوله : (ومن زنى بأمة فقتلها لزمه الحد والقيمة والخليفة يؤخذ بالقصاص والأموال لا بالحد . وإن كان دم . ولو كان زنى بأمة فقتلها لزمه الحد والقيمة معناه قتلها بفعل الزنا لأنه جنى جنايتين فيوفر على كل واحدة منهما حكمها. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يحد لأن تقرر ضمان القيمة سبب لملك الأمة وصار كما إذا اشتراها بعد ما زنى بها وهو على هذا الخلاف واعتراض سبب الملك قبل إقامة الحد يوجب سقوطه كما إذا ملك المسروق قبل القطع ولهما أنه ضمان قتل فلا . الملك لأن ضمان يوجب يوجبه إنما يوجبه في العين كما في هبة المسروق لا في منافع البضع لأنها استوفيت والملك يثبت مستنداً فلا يظهر في المستوفى لكونها معدومة، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا زنى بها فاذهب عينها حيث يجب عليه قيمتها ويسقط الحد لأن الملك هناك يثبت في الجثة العمياء وهي عين فأورث شبهة.
His statement: (And whoever commits zina with a slave woman and kills her, the hadd and the value are obligatory upon him, and the caliph seizes by retribution and property, not by hadd. And if it is blood... And if he committed zina with a slave woman and killed her, the hadd and the value are obligatory upon him.) The meaning is that he killed her by the act of zina, because he committed two crimes, so each one's ruling is applied fully. And from Abu Yusuf: He is not penalized because the establishment of guarantee of value is a cause for ownership of the slave woman, and it becomes like when he bought her after committing zina with her. This dispute is similar to the objection that the existence of a cause for ownership before the establishment of hadd necessitates its fall, like when one owns the stolen property before amputation. Their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) argument is that it is a guarantee for killing, so it does not necessitate ownership, because guarantee necessitates ownership only for the specific thing (ayn), as in the case of gifting the stolen property, not for the usufruct of the vulva because they have been already used, and ownership is established retroactively, so it does not appear in what has been used because it is non-existent. This is unlike the case where he committed zina with her and destroyed her eye; then the value is obligatory upon him and the hadd falls, because ownership there is established in the blind body, which is an ayn, so it creates a shubha.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a man commits zina with a slave woman and, in the process, kills her. The general ruling is that he must face the hadd penalty for zina and also pay the value (qimah) of the slave woman to her owner. The reason is that he has committed two separate crimes: zina and murder (or causing death). However, a distinction is made: if the killing is intentional, the caliph (ruler) can impose qisas (retribution) for the death, but hadd and property claims are separate. Abu Yusuf holds a dissenting view: he says that the hadd should fall because the obligation to pay the value becomes a cause for owning the slave woman, similar to if he bought her after zina. This is analogous to the rule that if a thief steals property and then purchases it before amputation, the hadd for theft is waived. However, the preferred view (that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as per the text 'ولهما') is that the guarantee for killing does not create ownership because it is compensation for loss of life, not for a specific object. The usufruct (the sexual act) is already consumed, so ownership cannot attach retroactively. Contrast this with the case of destroying an eye: there, the guarantee is for a specific body part (ayn), so it creates a shubha that negates hadd. Thus, in the killing case, both hadd and value remain.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīmah): The monetary value of the slave woman, payable by the offender to the owner.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt that can prevent the application of a fixed penalty (hadd).
عين (ʿayn): A specific physical object or property, as opposed to usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), a man who commits zina with a slave woman and kills her must:
A) Pay her value but no hadd.
B) Suffer hadd only, no value.
C) Suffer hadd and pay her value.
D) Be subject to qisas only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that the hadd for zina and the value for killing are both obligatory because the guarantee for killing does not create a shubha that would negate the hadd.
